The Dependency of Substances on an External Agent
Maturidi argues via contradiction that substances depend on an external agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that substances (jawāhir) are not self-sufficient but depend on an external creator, based on their potential for both corruption and reformation.

Core Idea
Substances' capacity for both corruption and reformation shows they are not self-subsistent.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise proof that the material substances we observe cannot be eternal or self-sufficient. He points to a simple but profound observation: every substance can undergo both corruption and reformation, which are opposites. Since a self-subsistent entity would not admit such change, substances must depend on something beyond themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's rhythm and logic come through.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key terms
Note the terms 'jawāhir' (substances), 'ifsād' (corruption), 'iṣlāḥ' (reformation), and 'bighayrihā' (by something other than itself). These are central to the argument.
3
Follow the logical structure
Observe how Maturidi sets up the proof: he states the premise (God's power), then presents the observation that substances admit opposites, and concludes they must be from another.
Look for the causal connection: contradiction implies external cause.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if substances were self-subsistent, would they be changeable? How does this argument refute materialism?
This argument is a classic kalam proof for a creator.
5
Test understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to reinforce the main conclusion.
The answer is directly from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنفسه فلا يُعجزه شيء، جل ثناؤه. وأيضا إنه ما من شيء مما ذكر من أنواع الجواهر إلا وقد يحتمل الإفساد والإصلاح جميعا؛ وذلك أيضا كله متضاد متدافع لا يحتمل الاجتماع للتعاون؛ ثبت أنها كانت على ما عليه بغيرها ; إذ كل شيء على جهة بنفسه لا يحتمل التغير ما دامت نفسه . وبالله العصمة.
By itself, nothing incapacitates Him, exalted is His praise. And also, there is nothing among the types of substances (jawāhir) mentioned except that it potentially admits both corruption and reformation; and all that is mutually contradictory and repellant, not admitting combination for cooperation; so it is established that they were as they are by something other than themselves; for everything that is by itself in a manner does not admit change as long as its self remains. And with Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a proof that substances (jawāhir) cannot be self-subsistent but require an external creator. The author argues that every substance has the potential for both corruption (ifsād) and reformation (iṣlāḥ), which are contradictory and mutually exclusive, so they cannot coexist independently. Therefore, they must be constituted by something other than themselves (bighayrihā). Furthermore, anything that is by its very nature (bi-nafsihi) unchangeable cannot admit change, but substances do change, proving they are not self-subsistent. This refutes the materialist claim that matter is eternal and self-sufficient.

Key Terms
جواهر (jawāhir)
الإفساد والإصلاح (al-ifsād wa-al-iṣlāḥ)
بغيرها (bighayrihā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage denies that substances change.
Correction: The passage does not deny change; it uses the fact that substances can change (specifically, can undergo corruption and reformation) to prove they are not self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about substances (jawāhir) from their potential for both corruption and reformation?
A) They are eternal and self-sufficient.
B) They are illusions with no reality.
C) They depend on something other than themselves.
D) They are created by themselves.

Key Takeaway
Substances depend on an external creator because they undergo contradictory changes that a self-subsistent entity would not admit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you see contradictory tendencies—desire for good and tendency towards corruption? How does this passage force you to admit that your own consistency is not from yourself but from Allah?
?
If you truly believed that every aspect of your existence is sustained by an external agent every moment, how would that change your reliance on your own plans and efforts?
?
The passage says anything that is by itself does not change. You experience constant change—moods, beliefs, actions. What does that imply about your self-sufficiency? Are you willing to acknowledge your utter dependence today?

The Argument from Universal Needs and Desires
Maturidi proves a creator from the presence of needs and desires in living beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument from universal needs and desires for the existence of a wise Creator, and why needs cannot originate from the beings themselves.

Core Idea
Needs and desires prove we are not self-sufficient—they point to a Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument drawn from everyday observation: every living being experiences needs and desires that drive them to seek fulfillment. This passage challenges us to consider whether these inherent dependencies could arise from the beings themselves or must point to an external Designer. As you read, notice how Maturidi systematically rules out self-generation and affirms the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before breaking it down.
The Arabic has some OCR issues; rely on the translation for clarity.
2
Identify Maturidi's three reasons
Find the three 'أوجه' (aspects/reasons) Maturidi gives to show that needs and desires cannot originate from the living beings themselves.
These reasons are the backbone of the argument.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Trace the argument: observation of needs → impossibility of self-generation → conclusion of an external Creator who provides.
Ask yourself: why is it impossible for a being to be the source of its own need?
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Understand that this argument affirms the existence of 'al-mudabbir' (the Manager/Designer) of the world, which is the central goal of the passage.
Maturidi is refuting those who deny a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن كل حي فيما يعاين مبني على الحاجات والشهوات التي تغلبهم وتقهرهم، ولولا ذلك ما احتاجوا إلى الأغذية. ثم كانت هي أسبابًا عند وجودها للغناء، لم يُحتمل أن يكون منها تهيج الشهوات وحدوث الحاجات. ولا يجوز أن تكون لأنفسها شهوات وحاجات، لأوجه: أحدها، أن القيام بالذات دون الغير دليل الغناء، لا يجوز أن يصير حاجة؛ ولأن ما كان لذاته على جهة لا يُحتمل زواله، وقد يقع لها الغناء من الغير؛ أو للإحالة أن يكون الشيء من حيث نفسه محوجاً إلى غير، بل إذا أحوج إلى غير من حيث نفسه تدوم الحاجة ما بقيت نفسه. فثبت أن هنالك غيراً أنشأهم على الحاجات وركب فيهم الشهوات، ثم أنشأ لهم ما به الغناء وقضاء الشهوات. فيكون بما أريد به نفي المدبّر للعالم تثبيته.
And also, every living being that is perceived is built upon needs (ḥājāt) and desires (shahawāt) that overcome and subdue them, and were it not for that, they would not need nourishment. Then they are, when present, causes of sufficiency (ghinā'), so it is not plausible that the arousing of desires and the occurrence of needs come from them. And it is not permissible that they have desires and needs by themselves, for aspects: one, that self-subsistence without another is proof of sufficiency, it is not permissible that it become a need; and because what is by its essence in a manner does not admit its removal, and sufficiency may come to it from another; or because it is impossible that a thing from its own self necessitate a need for another, rather if it necessitates a need for another from its own self, the need would persist as long as its self remains. So it is established that there is another who created them with needs and instilled in them desires, then created for them that by which is sufficiency and the fulfillment of desires. Thus what is intended by this is the affirmation of the existence of the manager of the world, not its denial.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of a creator based on the universal experience of needs and desires in living beings. Maturidi argues that every living being is subject to needs (ḥājāt) and desires (shahawāt) that compel them to seek nourishment. These needs and desires cannot originate from the beings themselves because they are self-sufficient in essence? No, rather they are dependent. He gives three reasons (أوجه): First, self-subsistence (qiyām bi-al-dhāt) without another is proof of sufficiency (ghinā'), so it cannot become needy. Second, what is by its essence unchanging cannot receive sufficiency from another if it were self-sufficient. Third, if a thing from its own self necessitated a need for another, the need would be perpetual. Since we observe temporary needs and desires, they must be created by an external, wise agent who designed them with needs and also provided means to fulfill them. Thus, the existence of a manager (mudabbir) is affirmed.

Key Terms
الحاجات (al-ḥājāt)
الشهوات (al-shahawāt)
الغناء (al-ghinā')
المدبّر (al-mudabbir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Living beings create their own needs and desires.
Correction: Maturidi argues that self-subsistence implies sufficiency, so needs cannot come from the being itself; they must be created externally.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why cannot living beings be the source of their own needs and desires?
A) Because needs and desires are illusions.
B) Because self-subsistence implies sufficiency, not need.
C) Because needs and desires are eternal.
D) Because they are imposed by society.

Key Takeaway
The universal experience of needs and desires leads to the affirmation of a wise Creator who designs beings with dependence and provides fulfillment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a desire you have that you cannot fulfill on your own. Does this desire point you to your dependence on Allah, or do you try to satisfy it independently? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to feel self-sufficient?
?
You have needs every day: food, water, companionship. When you fulfill them, do you attribute that sufficiency (ghina) to the Creator who provided the means, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? What would it look like to live in constant awareness that your needs and their fulfillment both come from Allah?
?
The passage says it is impossible for a thing to necessitate a need for another from its own self. Reflect on your own existence: Are there any aspects of your life where you act as if you are the source of your own needs and their satisfaction? How can you shift your mindset to acknowledge the Creator as the sole manager (mudabbir) of your affairs?

Two Proofs for a Wise Creator: Subjugation and Human Limitation
Maturidi argues that subjugation and human limitation prove a wise creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the subjugation of all things and human inability to argue for the existence of a wise creator.

Core Idea
The world's subjugation and our own limitations point to a Self-Subsistent Creator.

Maturidi presents two intertwined proofs for God's existence: everything in the world is subject to forces beyond itself, and even the most intelligent human cannot fully manage his own affairs. These observations lead to the conclusion that the world depends on a knowing, powerful, self-subsistent being. Let's examine how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two main arguments: first, the subjugation of all entities shows they cannot be self-managing; second, human limitation (even the wisest cannot manage his own subtle affairs) shows dependence. Find where each begins.
The first proof ends around 'ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليمًا' and the second begins with 'وبعد، لو خُلّي...'
3
Understand the conclusion
Both proofs converge on the same conclusion: the world cannot exist without an external Self-Subsistent, Knowing, Powerful being. Notice how Maturidi ties the two together at the end.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider what this argument tells us about God's attributes: He is a manager (mudabbir), knowing ('alīm), self-subsistent (qā'im bi-dhātihī), and powerful (qādir). How does this refute atheism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لا يُوجد شيء من أعيان العالم وصفاتها إلا مُسخّرًا به مذلّلاً بما لولا ذلك كان أهون عليه وألذ، نحو القرار الدائم والسير المتتابع مما كان به معاش أحد وما له المعاش فصار العالم بكليته بالمعنى الذي ذكرت ولا يجوز أن يكون المسخّر المذلل يملك التدبير حتى يكون به غنى الغير وقيامه، ولا يملك إزالة الذلة عن نفسه والسُّخرة. ثبت أن لكل ذلك مدبرا عليمًا علم وجوه حاجاتهم وغناهم فخلقهم على ذلك مع ما أحوج بعضاً إلى بعض في القيام والبقاء، على جهل كل منهم بالوجه الذي أخوج إلى غيره، وعجزه عن صرف وجه الحاجة عن نفسه. ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليمًا، على تدبيره جرى أمرهم. وبعد، لو خُلّي بين أعقل الخلق وأعظمهم تدبيرا وبين تقدير أحواله وأفعاله من الزمان والمكان فيما لطف منها لما احتمل وسعه، فمن دونه أحق. ثم لا يملك أحد منهم صرف قهر الزمان عن نفسه ولا إحاطة المكان. ثبت أن العالم على ما هو عليه، لا يجوز كونه به دون خارج من معناه في إحاطة الحاجة به، بل بالقائم بذاته، عالما قادرًا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Along with the fact that there is nothing among the entities of the world and their attributes except that it is subjugated and humiliated by it in a way that without that it would be easier and more pleasant for it, such as permanent rest and continuous motion which constitute the livelihood of one and the sustenance of another; thus the whole world is as I have described. And it is not permissible that the subjugated and humiliated possess management (tadbīr) so that through it the sufficiency of another and its subsistence occur, while it cannot remove humiliation and subjugation from itself. So it is established that for all that there is a knowing manager (mudabbir 'alīm) who knew the aspects of their needs and sufficiencies and created them accordingly, along with making some of them in need of others in duration and survival, while each of them is ignorant of the aspect by which he is in need of another, and powerless to avert the aspect of need from himself. So it is established that for all that there is a knowing manager, and by His management their affair proceeds. Furthermore, if the most intelligent of creation and the greatest in management were left to determine his own states and actions in terms of time and place regarding even the subtle aspects, it would not be within his capacity, so those beneath him are even more incapable. Then none of them can avert the domination of time from himself nor the encompassing of space. So it is established that the world as it is cannot be without an external entity that encompasses it with need, but rather it is by the Self-Subsistent, Knowing, Powerful. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents two complementary proofs for the existence of a wise creator. First, the subjugation (taskhīr) and humiliation (tadhlīl) of all entities in the world—nothing is absolute in its actions; everything is subjected to laws and serves purposes beyond itself. The subjugated cannot be the manager (mudabbir) because it cannot remove its own subjugation. So there must be a manager who knows all needs and created accordingly, making some dependent on others. Second, the limitation of even the most intelligent human: if the wisest person cannot fully manage his own subtle affairs, let alone others, and cannot control time or space, then the world cannot be self-managed. It must depend on a Self-Subsistent (qā'im bi-dhātihī), Knowing ('ālim), and Powerful (qādir) being. This refutes atheism and affirms a transcendent creator.

Key Terms
المسخّر (al-musakhkhar)
التدبير (al-tadbīr)
المدبّر (al-mudabbir)
القائم بذاته (al-qā'im bi-dhātihī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies human free will or reduces humans to puppets.
Correction: Maturidi's argument does not deny free will; it points to existential dependence. Humans have agency but are limited and cannot ultimately control time, space, or their own dependency. The subjugation refers to the inherent limitations of creatures, not the absence of choice.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the inability of even the most intelligent person to fully manage his own affairs?
A) That humans are not responsible for their actions.
B) That the world must depend on a Self-Subsistent, Knowing, Powerful being.
C) That management is an illusion.
D) That time and space are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that the subjugation of the world and human limitation both necessitate a wise, self-subsistent creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the areas of your life where you feel most in control—your schedule, health, or relationships. In what specific ways does this passage show that even that control is actually a form of subjugation and dependency? What would it mean for you to truly acknowledge that you are not the ultimate manager of your own life?
?
The passage says no human can avert the domination of time (qahr al-zamān) or the encompassing of space (ihāṭat al-makān). How does this confront your daily anxiety about the future or your attachment to material security? What would change if you lived with the constant awareness that you are completely dependent on the Self-Subsistent?
?
If you truly believed that every need and dependency you experience is placed by a knowing manager who knows exactly what you require, how would that change your approach to du'ā (supplication) and reliance (tawakkul)? What is one concrete step you can take today to live out that belief?

Maturidi's Dilemma on Eternal Matter
Maturidi introduces the dilemma for eternal matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dilemma that forces the claim of eternal matter into a choice that both lead to the necessity of a Creator.

Core Idea
Eternal matter either depends like the world or loses self-sufficiency—both require a Creator.

Imam Maturidi presents a sharp dilemma against those who assert the eternity of the world's matter. He forces them to choose: either this matter shares the contingent nature of the world, or it is utterly independent. Either way, it cannot escape dependence on a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two branches
Read the first part of the passage and note the two possibilities Maturidi sets up for the eternal matter: either it is of the same substance as the world or it is entirely different.
This is the structure of a dilemma.
2
Examine the first branch
If matter is of the same substance, it shares the world's incapability and need, proving its temporality and dependence on another.
This makes matter contingent like all creation.
3
Examine the second branch
If matter is different and self-sufficient, then the world's transformation by accidents voids that self-sufficiency, and management must come from a wise being.
Even in this scenario, a Creator is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من يقول بقدم طينة العالم: فإما أن كانت من جوهر هذه المعاني فيلحقها ما يلحق العالم ويُظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه بغيره، فيلزم فيها ما يلزم في غيرها؛ أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية لا تمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى غير به تأمل غناها وقوتها. فإما أن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة، محلّة لكل شهوة، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير، فبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم؛ أو كانت بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة، فظهر بالفعل؛ وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى. ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل ـ نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطفة، وكل حيوان في النُّطَف أو البيض، والعصف في الحب، والشجر في النواة، وكذلك كل الجواهر، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك. على أنه يجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك، إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم.
Then as for one who says that the clay (ṭīna) of the world is eternal: either it was of the substance of these meanings, so what applies to the world applies to it, and its substance shows its incapability and needs, which are proofs of the world's temporality and its being by another, so what applies to other things applies to it; or it was outside this substance, sufficient and powerful, untouched by needs and not obstructed by desires that drive to contrivances, by considering its sufficiency and power. Then either the world came into being through it by accidents affecting it and transforming it from its original state into these needs and desires, so that it becomes in its substance susceptible to every need, locus of every desire, capable of change and transformation, thus all attributes of sufficiency and power are voided, and it becomes the source of needs and mother of desires, so its management must be attributed to a wise and knowing one, as is necessary for all the world; or it remained as it is, but the world was in it potentially, then appeared actually; and that is the opinion of the proponents of hyle (hayūlā). Then the dissolution of everything in the world from something being in something potentially when it emerges from it actually—as they say of the breath being in the sperm, and every animal in sperm or eggs, and the bird in the egg, and the tree in the seed, and likewise all substances, and similarly for them sustenance in food and growth and so on—indicates that the matter of the clay (ṭīna) they claim and the matter of hyle (hayūlā) must be likewise, for they are the origin of the entire world.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that the world's matter (ṭīna) or prime matter (hayūlā) is eternal and self-sufficient. Maturidi presents a dilemma: either the eternal matter is of the same nature as the world's substances (jawāhir) or it is different. If it is the same, then it shares the world's contingency (ḥudūth) and dependence on another. If it is different and self-sufficient, then two possibilities arise: (1) The world was produced from it by accidental changes that transformed it, in which case it loses its self-sufficiency and needs a wise creator. (2) The world was contained in it potentially and then actualized, which is the view of the proponents of hyle (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā). But this view is refuted by the fact that everything in the world that exists potentially in something else—like a plant in a seed—needs an external agent to actualize it. Therefore, the eternal matter itself, if it contained the world potentially, would require an external agent to bring it into actuality. Thus, the supposed eternal matter is also contingent and dependent on a creator. This closes the argument that the world's material origin cannot be eternal and self-sufficient.

Key Terms
طينة العالم (ṭīnat al-‘ālam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal matter is completely self-sufficient and independent of any creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even if matter is independent, its transformation into the world (via accidents) proves it becomes dependent and requires a wise creator.

Checkpoint
According to the first branch of Maturidi's dilemma, what happens if the eternal matter is of the same substance as the world?
A) It becomes self-sufficient.
B) It becomes temporal and dependent like the world.
C) It remains eternal and unchanged.
D) It transforms into a different substance.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma shows that whether eternal matter is like the world or self-sufficient, it cannot escape the need for a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this dilemma, did you assume that matter could exist independently of God? How does this argument make you reconsider your assumptions about the universe's origin?
?
If you were to encounter someone who believes matter is eternal and self-sufficient, how would this dilemma challenge their view? What would you say to them?
?
This argument shows that everything that shows need or change requires a Manager. Look at your own life: what needs or changes in your character point you to your need for God's guidance and management?

Refuting Hyle: The Argument from Potentiality
Maturidi refutes the hyle theory using the potentiality-actuality argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if the world existed in eternal matter potentially, then an external agent is required to actualize it, proving matter is not self-sufficient.

Core Idea
Potential existence always needs an external actualizer—prime matter is no exception.

Maturidi now addresses the classic hyle (hayūlā) theory: that the world was contained potentially in eternal matter. He counters with an evident truth from nature: everything that exists in potentiality requires an external agent to bring it into actuality. This applies to the world's matter itself, undermining its claim to eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hyle position
Read the passage where Maturidi presents the view that the world existed potentially (bi-l-quwwa) in eternal matter and then appeared actually (bi-l-fi‘l).
This is the theory of prime matter.
2
Analyze the analogy
Maturidi gives examples: breath in sperm, animal in sperm/eggs, tree in seed. All these need an external agent to actualize.
These are everyday observations.
3
Apply the analogy to hyle
The same reasoning applies to the claimed eternal matter: if it contained the world potentially, it must have needed an external agent to actualize it.
Hence, matter is contingent, not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من يقول بقدم طينة العالم: فإما أن كانت من جوهر هذه المعاني فيلحقها ما يلحق العالم ويُظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه بغيره، فيلزم فيها ما يلزم في غيرها؛ أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية لا تمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى غير به تأمل غناها وقوتها. فإما أن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة، محلّة لكل شهوة، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير، فبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم؛ أو كانت بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة، فظهر بالفعل؛ وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى. ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل ـ نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطفة، وكل حيوان في النُّطَف أو البيض، والعصف في الحب، والشجر في النواة، وكذلك كل الجواهر، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك. على أنه يجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك، إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم.
Then as for one who says that the clay (ṭīna) of the world is eternal: either it was of the substance of these meanings, so what applies to the world applies to it, and its substance shows its incapability and needs, which are proofs of the world's temporality and its being by another, so what applies to other things applies to it; or it was outside this substance, sufficient and powerful, untouched by needs and not obstructed by desires that drive to contrivances, by considering its sufficiency and power. Then either the world came into being through it by accidents affecting it and transforming it from its original state into these needs and desires, so that it becomes in its substance susceptible to every need, locus of every desire, capable of change and transformation, thus all attributes of sufficiency and power are voided, and it becomes the source of needs and mother of desires, so its management must be attributed to a wise and knowing one, as is necessary for all the world; or it remained as it is, but the world was in it potentially, then appeared actually; and that is the opinion of the proponents of hyle (hayūlā). Then the dissolution of everything in the world from something being in something potentially when it emerges from it actually—as they say of the breath being in the sperm, and every animal in sperm or eggs, and the bird in the egg, and the tree in the seed, and likewise all substances, and similarly for them sustenance in food and growth and so on—indicates that the matter of the clay (ṭīna) they claim and the matter of hyle (hayūlā) must be likewise, for they are the origin of the entire world.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that the world's matter (ṭīna) or prime matter (hayūlā) is eternal and self-sufficient. Maturidi presents a dilemma: either the eternal matter is of the same nature as the world's substances (jawāhir) or it is different. If it is the same, then it shares the world's contingency (ḥudūth) and dependence on another. If it is different and self-sufficient, then two possibilities arise: (1) The world was produced from it by accidental changes that transformed it, in which case it loses its self-sufficiency and needs a wise creator. (2) The world was contained in it potentially and then actualized, which is the view of the proponents of hyle (aṣḥāb al-hayūlā). But this view is refuted by the fact that everything in the world that exists potentially in something else—like a plant in a seed—needs an external agent to actualize it. Therefore, the eternal matter itself, if it contained the world potentially, would require an external agent to bring it into actuality. Thus, the supposed eternal matter is also contingent and dependent on a creator. This closes the argument that the world's material origin cannot be eternal and self-sufficient.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayūlā)
القوة (al-quwwa)
الفعل (al-fi‘l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use as evidence against the hyle theory?
A) The world is too complex to be potential.
B) Examples from nature where potential things require an external agent to become actual.
C) The eternal matter is not real.
D) The hyle theory is self-contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Even if the world existed potentially in eternal matter, an external Creator is still needed to bring it into actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that nothing in existence becomes actual without an external cause. Is there an area of your life where you are relying on your own latent potential to actualize itself, without actively depending on Allah? What would it mean to consciously attribute your progress to Him?
?
Maturidi uses everyday examples like a seed becoming a tree. When you see growth in nature or in yourself, do you truly see Allah’s hand in the process, or do you take it for granted? What habit could you adopt to turn these observations into worship?
?
If potentiality always requires an actualizer, then your very existence and every change in your life is a sign of Allah’s ongoing creation. How does this change your perspective on the next difficulty you face? Will you see it as a call to turn to the Creator, or as a random event?

Proof of a Wise Creator from Creatures' Incapacity
Maturidi proves a wise manager from creatures' ignorance and need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues for the existence of a wise Creator from the incapacity of creatures to manage their own existence and needs.

Core Idea
Creatures' incapacity proves a wise manager.

This passage from Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument for the existence of a wise Creator. It begins by observing that every creature is ignorant of the precise manner in which it depends on others and is incapable of removing its own needs. This leads to the conclusion that there must be an all-wise director. Then, Maturidi sharpens the argument by showing that even the most intelligent beings cannot fully manage their own subtle affairs in time and space, let alone lesser beings. Thus, the world requires a Self-Subsisting Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: two premises followed by a conclusion.
The passage uses the phrase 'ثبت أن' which means 'it is established that'.
2
Identify the main argument
The argument has two parts: first from every creature's ignorance and incapacity, second from the inability of even the most intelligent to manage their own affairs. Both lead to the necessity of a wise manager.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The final conclusion is that the world cannot be without a Self-Subsisting, Knowing, Powerful Creator. Note how the argument does not rely on design alone but on inherent neediness.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' is a declaration of reliance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في القيام والبقاء، على جهل كل منهم بالوجه الذي أخوج إلى غيره، وعجزه عن صرف وجه الحاجة عن نفسه. ثبت أن لذلك كله مديرا عليمًا، على تدبيره جرى أمرهم. وبعد، لو خُلّى بين أعقل الخلق وأعظمهم تدبيرا وبين تقدير أحواله وأفعاله من الزمان والمكان فيما لطف منها لما احتمل وسعه، فمن دونه أحق. ثم لا يملك أحد منهم صرف قهر الزمان عن نفسه ولا إحاطة المكان. ثبت أن العالم على ما هو عليه، لا يجوز كونه به دون خارج من معناه في إحاطة الحاجة به، بل بالقائم بذاته، عالما قادرًا. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
In standing and remaining, each of them is ignorant of the manner that necessitates another, and is incapable of turning away the face of need from itself. So it is established that all of this has a wise director, by whose management their affair proceeds. Moreover, if the most intelligent of creation and the greatest in management were left alone with the estimation of his states and actions, of time and place, in what is subtle among them, it would not be within his capacity; so those below him are more deserving. Then not one of them can avert the compulsion of time from himself nor the encompassing of place. It is established that the world, as it is, cannot be with it without something external to its meaning in the encompassing of need upon it, but rather by the Self-Subsisting, Knowing, Powerful. And there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the existence of a wise creator based on the incapacity of all creatures. Maturidi begins by observing that every being, in its existence and duration (القيام والبقاء), is ignorant of the exact manner in which it is necessitated by other things, and is unable to remove its own needs. Therefore, there must be a wise manager (مديرا عليمًا) who administers all affairs. He then argues that even the most intelligent and capable human, if left alone to manage his own subtle states and actions in time and space, would be unable to do so; hence those less capable are even more unable. Furthermore, no creature can ward off the dominance of time or the encompassing of space. Thus the world, as it is, cannot exist without a creator external to its own nature—one who is self-subsisting, knowing, and powerful. The passage ends with the formula 'لا قوة إلا بالله' (there is no power except by Allah), which is a common expression of reliance on God. The argument moves from the observable incapacity and neediness of creatures to the necessity of a transcendent director.

Key Terms
القيام
البقاء
مديرا عليمًا
تدبيره
القاهر الزمان
إحاطة المكان
القائم بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof relies on ignorance rather than positive knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi argues from manifest incapacity and need, not from mere ignorance. The evidence is observable: creatures cannot manage their own affairs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence that the world has a wise director?
A) The intricate design of the world
B) The inability of any creature to manage its own affairs fully
C) The agreement of all philosophers
D) The eternity of the world

Key Takeaway
Creatures' inherent neediness and incapacity to manage their own affairs necessitate the existence of a wise, self-subsisting Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that even the most intelligent creature cannot manage his own subtle states. In what area of your life have you been acting as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that every detail is managed by Allah? Will you confess your incapacity to Him today and ask for His guidance in that area?
?
The passage says no one can avert the compulsion of time. How does the reality of aging and the approach of death affect your daily priorities? Are you living as if you have unlimited time, or are you actively preparing for the moment you will stand before your Creator?
?
Maturidi argues from your own ignorance and need. Have you ever felt the weight of your own helplessness—perhaps in a moment of illness or loss? How does that feeling of dependence change your relationship with Allah? What specific action will you take this week to consciously rely on Him?

Refutation of Eternal Prime Matter and Hyle
Maturidi refutes eternal prime matter and hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the theories of eternal prime matter and hyle by demonstrating that even if they existed, they would still be contingent and dependent on a Creator.

Core Idea
If prime matter were eternal, it would still need a Creator.

Imam Maturidi now addresses a key challenge to the doctrine of creation ex nihilo: the idea that the world is made from an eternal substance. He systematically dismantles this view, showing that such a substance would have to be either like the world or unlike it—and in either case, it fails to explain the world's contingency. This passage uses a disjunctive argument to expose the inherent weakness of both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a highly systematic, almost mathematical style.
2
Identify the two main possibilities
Maturidi splits the claim of eternal clay into two cases: either it is of the same substance as transient things (A), or it is external and self-sufficient (B). Identify these in the text.
The letters (أ) and (ب) mark these branches.
3
Examine the sub-argument for case B
Case B is further divided: either the world came about through changes to that clay (أأ), or the world existed potentially in it and became actual (بب). This sub-argument leads to two different refutations.
The second sub-case (بب) is the view of the supporters of hyle.
4
Understand the final analogy
Maturidi concludes by drawing an analogy: everything that exists potentially in another thing perishes when actualized (e.g., the human in sperm, the tree in the seed). By the same logic, the alleged eternal prime matter and hyle must also perish, as they are the origin of all such potentialities.
This refutes the hyle theory by showing its own logical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من يقول بقدم طينة العالم : (أ) فإما أن كانت من جوهر هذه المعاني فيلحقها ما يلحق العالم ويظهر جوهرها عجزها وحاجاتها، وهما دليلا حدث العالم وكونه بغيره، فيلزم فيها ما يلزم في غيرها؛ (ب) أو كانت خارجة من هذا الجوهر غنية قوية لاتمسها الحاجات ولا يعترضها الشهوات الباعثة على الحيل المفزعة إلى غير به تأمل غناها وقوتها. (أأ) فإما أن كان العالم بها بأن اعترضت بها العوارض وانقلبت بجوهرها عما كانت عليه فصارت إلى هذه الحاجات والشهوات، فصارت بجوهرها محتملة لكل حاجة، محلة لكل شهوة، متمكنة للاستحالة والتغير، فبطل عنها جميع أوصاف الغنى والقوة، وصارت أصل الحاجات وأم الشهوات، فلزم انصراف تدبيرها إلى حكيم عليم على ما لزم ذلك في جميع العالم؛ (بب) أو كانت بحالها، لكن العالم كان فيها بقوة، فظهر بالفعل؛ وذلك هو قول أصحاب الهيولى. ثم دل تلف جميع ما في العالم من كون شيء في شيء بالقوة إذا خرج منه بالفعل ـ نحو ما يقولون من كون النسمة في النطفة، وكل حيوان في النطف أو البيض، والعصف في الحب، والشجر في النواة، وكذلك كل الجواهر، ومثله عندهم البقاء في الأغذية والنماء ونحو ذلك. على أنه يجب أن يكون أمر الطينة التي قالوا بها وأمر الهيولى كذلك، إذ هما الأصل لجميع العالم.
Then as for those who say the world's clay (طينة) is eternal: (A) Either it is of the substance of these meanings, so what affects the world affects it, and its substance shows its incapacity and needs, which are the two proofs of the world's temporality and its existence by another, so what applies to other things applies to it; (B) Or it is external to this substance, rich, powerful, untouched by needs and not affected by desires that impel to ruses [؟] reflecting on its richness and power. (AA) Either the world was by it through accidents intervening and it turned in its substance from what it was, so it became to these needs and desires, thus its substance became susceptible to every need, a place for every desire, capable of change and alteration, so all attributes of richness and power were annulled from it, and it became the origin of needs and the mother of desires, so its management must be referred to a wise knowing one, as that applies to the whole world; (BB) Or it remained as it was, but the world was in it potentially, so it appeared actually; that is the statement of the supporters of hyle (الهيولى). Then the perishing of everything in the world, from the existence of one thing in another potentially when it comes out of it actually—like their statement of the human being in the sperm, and every animal in sperms or eggs, and the bird in the egg, and the tree in the pit, and likewise all substances, and similarly with them the continuance in foods and growth and the like—proves that the matter of the clay they assert and the matter of hyle must be likewise, since they are the origin for the entire world.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who claim the world's prime matter (طينة) is eternal. Maturidi presents a disjunctive argument: The eternal clay is either (أ) of the same nature as transient things, in which case it shares their temporality and need for a creator, or (ب) it is foreign to that nature, self-sufficient and powerful. The second possibility is further subdivided: (أأ) The world might have come about through accidents that changed this eternal matter, making it subject to need and desire, thus annulling its supposed self-sufficiency and requiring a wise director—just as the rest of the world does. Or (بب) the eternal matter remained unchanged, but the world existed in it potentially and then appeared actually; this is the view of the supporters of hyle (الهيولى). Maturidi then argues that everything in the world that exists potentially in another thing perishes when it becomes actual (e.g., the human being in the sperm, the tree in the seed). Therefore, by analogy, the alleged eternal prime matter and hyle must also perish, since they are the origin of all these potential beings. Thus, the theories of eternal matter are refuted.

Key Terms
طينة
قدم
جوهر
الهيولى
بالقوة
بالفعل
تلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is accepting the existence of eternal matter as a possibility.
Correction: He is actually showing that even if it existed, it would still be contingent and dependent on a Creator, thus refuting its eternity.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the fact that things existing potentially in other things perish when actualized?
A) The world is eternal
B) The prime matter must also perish, since it contains the world potentially
C) Only the hyle is eternal
D) The prime matter is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
By analogy with all other potentialities, the supposed eternal prime matter must also be temporal and dependent on a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that even the supposed eternal 'prime matter' is subject to need and change. Is there any area of your life where you treat something as eternal—a habit, a relationship, a career—as if it will always sustain you? How can you break that illusion today and turn back to the only Eternal?
?
The passage shows that potentiality itself is a sign of perishing. What potential in your own life—a skill, a dream, a relationship—are you relying on for future happiness without acknowledging that its very existence is a gift from Allah and could vanish at any moment? What step will you take this week to surrender that hope to Him?
?
If the very foundation of the physical world is contingent, what excuse do you have for pride or self-sufficiency? Think of one specific moment in your day when you felt self-reliant. How can you replace that feeling with direct, intentional dependence on Allah?

The First Principle Must Perish: Refuting Qarmatian Emanation
Maturidi refutes Qarmatian emanation by applying the potentiality-actuality principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qarmatian theory of emanation logically requires that the first principle perishes when the world is actualized.

Core Idea
Everything potential must perish when actualized.

Maturidi begins his refutation by taking the Qarmatians' own assumptions—that the first originator contains the entire world potentially—and showing that this leads to a contradiction. If the world comes forth from it, the first originator must change or perish. This passage is the core of the logical attack.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical progression: statement of Qarmatian view, application of rule, conclusion.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the phrase 'حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل'. This is the principle that whatever exists potentially in something must perish when actualized.
This is a universal logical rule.
3
See the consequence
Notice how Maturidi applies this to the Qarmatian first principle: it must perish (يتلف). Also note the additional point about the whole being known only through the part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يلزم القرامطة في قولهم: إن المبدع الأول فيه جميع العالم مبروزاً تستمد منه النفس الكل فتمد الهيولى، ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل. وقد صيّروا جميعا الوجودَ للحال دليل الأولية، لكن الأول جوهر الكل، والثاني جوهر الجزء، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء، إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس. وبالله التوفيق. وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بينا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي الأدلة للحدث لذلك الأصل، إذ صار محتملاً للتلف والفناء. وذلك أيضاً يلزم القرامطة، إذ يجعلونه أبديا، وإن كانوا يقولون: كان بعد أن لم يكن بالإبداع. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ودليل جهل من فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون منه كما ذكرت من جهل النطَف والحبوب وغيرها. فيلزم ذلك في الهيولى والطينة و ما قالوا؛ ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به. ولكن إن كان فهو بمن علم ما يكون، فيجعل أصله مبروزاً فيه بالقوة. ثم يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو. وفي ذلك القولُ بالتوحيد وأنه منشئ ذلك كله؛ ليكون به كل شيء يكون: إما على ما قالوا، أو أن يكون محدثًا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من أصول أو ابتداء، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل، فيه كل شيء مبروز قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين.
And likewise it is necessary against the Qarmatians in their statement: that the first originator contains the entire world made manifest, from which the universal soul draws and supplies the hyle, and from it comes the composition of the world—that the first must perish; for this is the truth about everything that exists in something else potentially when it appears actually. And they have made all existence for the state a proof of priority, but the first is the substance of the whole, and the second is the substance of the part, and the substance of the whole has become known by the substance of the part, since it is not something accessible to anyone by sense. And with Allah is success. And when this is established, all that we have explained of events and needs, which are the proofs of temporality for that origin, are established, since it becomes subject to perishing and annihilation. And that also applies to the Qarmatians, for they make it eternal, even though they say: it came to be after not being, by origination. And there is no power except by Allah. And the proof of ignorance of one who has something potentially in it concerning the states of what is in it and who is in it and what will come from it, as I mentioned regarding the ignorance of sperms and grains and others. So that applies to the hyle and the clay and what they said; and it is necessary that what they said has no management, nor was anything of that by it. But if it is, then it is by one who knows what will be, so He makes its origin manifest in it potentially. Then it appears actually by means of the materials and places that He has made for it to grow. And in that is the affirmation of tawhid and that He is the originator of all that; so that everything that comes to be may be by Him: either as they said, or that He creates everything that comes to be always as He wills, from origins or from ex nihilo, or however He wills, as the people of tawhid say—and Allah is capable of originating an origin in which everything is manifest, capable of initiating everything as He wills without potential manifestation nor actual emergence, but by predetermination and formation (التقدير والتكوين).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qarmatians and a reaffirmation of tawhid. Maturidi first applies the same argument from potentiality to the Qarmatian emanation theory, in which the first principle (المبدع الأول) contains the entire world potentially, and the universal soul mediates to actualize it. If so, the first principle must perish (يتلف) just as all potential things do when actualized. The Qarmatians make the first principle the substance of the whole, and its derivative the substance of the part, but the whole is known only through the part—which is not perceived by the senses. Therefore, their theory collapses. If the Qarmatian first principle is eternal, yet they claim it originated from non-existence, then it is still subject to perishing. Maturidi then connects this to his earlier argument about the ignorance of sperm and seeds: whoever posits an origin containing everything potentially is ignorant of what that origin contains and what will come from it—just as one is ignorant of what a sperm will become. Thus, the hyle, clay, and Qarmatian first principle have no inherent management; if they have any, it must be from a wise creator. The correct view is tawhid: God is the originator of all, either as the Qarmatians claim (impossibly) or as the people of tawhid hold—that God directly creates everything ex nihilo or from origins as He wills, without needing potentiality or actualization, but by His predetermined decree and formation (التقدير والتكوين).

Key Terms
القرامطة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qarmatian first principle is eternal and unchanging.
Correction: If it contains the world potentially, its actualization necessitates change and perishing, so it cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what must happen to the Qarmatian first principle when the world is actualized from it?
A) It remains unchanged
B) It must perish
C) It becomes the universal soul
D) It becomes the hyle

Key Takeaway
The Qarmatian first principle must perish if the world is actualized from it, exposing the incoherence of their emanation theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Qarmatians thought the first principle could contain the world and then actualize it without changing. Do you ever hold beliefs about God that, upon reflection, imply He undergoes change or is dependent on creation? How does this passage challenge you to purify your understanding of God's utter self-sufficiency?
?
Maturidi argues that what is potential in something, when actualized, causes the original to perish. If you consider your own life, are there ways you have 'actualized' potentials in ways that have caused a previous state of yourself to 'perish'? What does that teach you about the necessity of God's unchangeability?
?
The passage states that the substance of the whole is known only through the substance of the part. In your relationship with Allah, do you ever reduce Him to your limited experiences? Commit to one practice this week that reminds you that He is beyond all sensory knowledge.

Ignorance of Potentiality: The Qarmatian Flaw Exposed
Maturidi uses analogy of sperms/grains to show Qarmatian theory implies ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the analogy of sperm and seeds to argue that the Qarmatian theory of potentiality implies ignorance, not knowledge.

Core Idea
Having potential does not grant knowledge of future actualities.

Maturidi now reinforces his refutation by connecting to an earlier argument. He asserts that one who possesses something potentially is ignorant of its future states, just as one is ignorant of what a sperm will become. This same ignorance applies to the Qarmatian hyle and clay, demonstrating that their theory lacks any inherent design or management.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi first resumes the previous point, then introduces the analogy of sperms and grains.
2
Understand the analogy
The analogy shows that the Qarmatian first principle, containing everything potentially, is comparable to a sperm: it does not know what it will become.
This is a proof of ignorance (دليل جهل).
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the Qarmatian theory posits ignorance at its core, it cannot account for management or design in the universe. The true source of management must be an external wise being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يلزم القرامطة في قولهم: إن المبدع الأول فيه جميع العالم مبروزاً تستمد منه النفس الكل فتمد الهيولى، ومنه تركيب العالم - أن يتلف الأول؛ إذ هذا حق كل شيء في شيء بالقوة يظهر بالفعل. وقد صيّروا جميعا الوجودَ للحال دليل الأولية، لكن الأول جوهر الكل، والثاني جوهر الجزء، وقد صار جوهر الكل معروفا بجوهر الجزء، إذ ليس مما يبلغه أحد بالحس. وبالله التوفيق. وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت جميع ما بينا من الحوادث والحاجات التي هي الأدلة للحدث لذلك الأصل، إذ صار محتملاً للتلف والفناء. وذلك أيضاً يلزم القرامطة، إذ يجعلونه أبديا، وإن كانوا يقولون: كان بعد أن لم يكن بالإبداع. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ودليل جهل من فيه ما فيه بالقوة بأحوال ما فيه ومن فيه وما يكون منه كما ذكرت من جهل النطَف والحبوب وغيرها. فيلزم ذلك في الهيولى والطينة و ما قالوا؛ ولزم أن ليس لما قالوا تدبير، ولا كان شيء من ذلك به. ولكن إن كان فهو بمن علم ما يكون، فيجعل أصله مبروزاً فيه بالقوة. ثم يظهر بالفعل بما جعل له من المواد والأمكنة التي بها تنمو. وفي ذلك القولُ بالتوحيد وأنه منشئ ذلك كله؛ ليكون به كل شيء يكون: إما على ما قالوا، أو أن يكون محدثًا لكل شيء يكون أبدا على ما يشاء أن يكون من أصول أو ابتداء، أو كيف شاء على ما يقوله أهل التوحيد وإذ الله سبحانه قادر على إنشاء أصل، فيه كل شيء مبروز قادر على ابتداء كل شيء كما شاء من غير بروز بالقوة ولا خروج بالفعل، ولكن بالتقدير والتكوين.
And likewise it is necessary against the Qarmatians in their statement: that the first originator contains the entire world made manifest, from which the universal soul draws and supplies the hyle, and from it comes the composition of the world—that the first must perish; for this is the truth about everything that exists in something else potentially when it appears actually. And they have made all existence for the state a proof of priority, but the first is the substance of the whole, and the second is the substance of the part, and the substance of the whole has become known by the substance of the part, since it is not something accessible to anyone by sense. And with Allah is success. And when this is established, all that we have explained of events and needs, which are the proofs of temporality for that origin, are established, since it becomes subject to perishing and annihilation. And that also applies to the Qarmatians, for they make it eternal, even though they say: it came to be after not being, by origination. And there is no power except by Allah. And the proof of ignorance of one who has something potentially in it concerning the states of what is in it and who is in it and what will come from it, as I mentioned regarding the ignorance of sperms and grains and others. So that applies to the hyle and the clay and what they said; and it is necessary that what they said has no management, nor was anything of that by it. But if it is, then it is by one who knows what will be, so He makes its origin manifest in it potentially. Then it appears actually by means of the materials and places that He has made for it to grow. And in that is the affirmation of tawhid and that He is the originator of all that; so that everything that comes to be may be by Him: either as they said, or that He creates everything that comes to be always as He wills, from origins or from ex nihilo, or however He wills, as the people of tawhid say—and Allah is capable of originating an origin in which everything is manifest, capable of initiating everything as He wills without potential manifestation nor actual emergence, but by predetermination and formation (التقدير والتكوين).
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qarmatians and a reaffirmation of tawhid. Maturidi first applies the same argument from potentiality to the Qarmatian emanation theory, in which the first principle (المبدع الأول) contains the entire world potentially, and the universal soul mediates to actualize it. If so, the first principle must perish (يتلف) just as all potential things do when actualized. The Qarmatians make the first principle the substance of the whole, and its derivative the substance of the part, but the whole is known only through the part—which is not perceived by the senses. Therefore, their theory collapses. If the Qarmatian first principle is eternal, yet they claim it originated from non-existence, then it is still subject to perishing. Maturidi then connects this to his earlier argument about the ignorance of sperm and seeds: whoever posits an origin containing everything potentially is ignorant of what that origin contains and what will come from it—just as one is ignorant of what a sperm will become. Thus, the hyle, clay, and Qarmatian first principle have no inherent management; if they have any, it must be from a wise creator. The correct view is tawhid: God is the originator of all, either as the Qarmatians claim (impossibly) or as the people of tawhid hold—that God directly creates everything ex nihilo or from origins as He wills, without needing potentiality or actualization, but by His predetermined decree and formation (التقدير والتكوين).

Key Terms
القرامطة
التلف
الإبداع
النطَف
الحبوب
الهيولى
الطينة
التدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qarmatian first principle, containing all potentialities, knows what will come out of it.
Correction: Maturidi shows that potentiality implies ignorance, not knowledge, just as a sperm does not know it will become a human.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi compare the Qarmatian first principle to in order to show its ignorance?
A) A tree
B) A sperm or grain
C) A universal soul
D) A hyle

Key Takeaway
The Qarmatian theory of emanation relies on a principle of potentiality that implies ignorance, not knowledge, and thus fails to account for order and management in the universe.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you assume that potential (e.g., talents, resources, plans) guarantees knowledge of outcomes? How has this assumption led you to neglect seeking actual guidance from Allah?
?
This lesson shows that claiming knowledge from mere potentiality is ignorance. Are there beliefs you hold about yourself or others based on 'what could be' rather than reality? How can you repent from such speculation?
?
The Qarmatians thought their first principle could manage the world, but Maturidi proves it cannot. How often do you rely on your own 'potential' to manage your affairs instead of turning to Allah, the All-Knowing? What one concrete change will you make today to acknowledge His sole management?

Affirmation of Tawhid: Creation by Predetermination and Formation
Maturidi affirms tawhid and describes divine creation by predetermination and formation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Islamic doctrine of creation: God creates directly by His will, using predetermination and formation, not through potentiality and actuality.

Core Idea
God creates by predetermination and formation, not by emanation.

After demolishing the Qarmatian theory, Maturidi presents the positive doctrine of tawhid. He contrasts the Qarmatian view with the true understanding: God is capable of originating everything directly, either from existing origins or from nothing, as He wills. Creation occurs through His predetermination (التقدير) and formation (التكوين), not through a process of potentiality and actualization. This lesson establishes the Islamic alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional statement at the beginning and the final affirmation.
2
Understand the conditional
Maturidi says that if there is any management in the Qarmatian origin, it must be from a wise creator who knows the future and places potentiality in things.
This concedes the possibility that the Qarmatian view could be salvaged, but only if it leads to a wise creator.
3
Learn the two options
Maturidi presents two options: either as the Qarmatians say (false) or as the people of tawhid say (true).
4
Grasp the true doctrine
The true doctrine is that God creates everything directly, without potentiality or actualization, but by predetermination and formation.

Source Text

?

?

?
The Qarmatians believed in a passive process of creation. Is there any area of your life where you act as if Allah's role is passive—just setting the world in motion and leaving it—rather than actively creating every moment? What step will you take tonight to acknowledge His ongoing, direct creation?

Allah Exists Without Place and Does Not Change
Maturidi proves Allah's timelessness and spacelessness through logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Allah's existence is independent of place and time, and why change is impossible for the Eternal.

Core Idea
Allah was without place and remains so; change proves temporality, not eternity.

This short but profound passage from Imam Maturidi establishes a foundational principle of Islamic theology: Allah is not confined by space or time. He existed before any place existed, and He will remain even if all places cease. The argument then links change with contingency, proving that the Eternal does not change. Read carefully and follow the logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage softly, then look at the literal translation. Absorb the density of Maturidi's style.
The Arabic is concise; each phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the core assertion and argument
Maturidi begins with a principle: Allah was without place. Then he argues that places can vanish while Allah remains as He was. Note the logical reasoning: change implies contingency, so Allah cannot change.
Look for the phrase 'إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث' — this is the key evidence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for understanding Allah's nature: He is not affected by time or space. This refutes anthropomorphism and establishes tanzih (transcendence).
Compare with created things that change and are in places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : الأصل فيه أن الله سبحانه كان ولا مكان، وجائز ارتفاع الأمكنة وبقاؤه على ما كان فهو على ما كان، وكان على ما عليه الآن. جل عن التغيّر والزوال والاستحالة والبطلان؛ إذ ذلك أمارات الحدث التي بها عُرف حدث العالم ودلالة احتمال الفناء؛ إذ لا فرق بين الزوال من حال إلى حال - بعلم أن حاله الأولى لم تكن لذاته، إذ لا يُحتمل زوالُها ما لزمت ذاته - وبين ذاته التي ليست لذاته، لما احتمل هو قبول الأعراض وانتقال الأحوال. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in this is that Allah, exalted, was and there was no place, and it is possible for places to cease to exist while He remains as He was, so He is as He was, and He was as He is now. He is exalted above change, passing, transformation, and annihilation; for these are signs of temporality by which the temporality of the world is known and an indication of the possibility of perishing; for there is no difference between passing from one state to another—knowing that his first state was not essential to him, since it is not possible for it to pass if it were essential to him—and between an essence that is not essential to him, because it is susceptible to accepting accidents and transition of states. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a proof of Allah's existence without place and His changelessness. Maturidi begins with the foundational principle: Allah existed before any place existed, and even if all places cease, He remains as He always was. He is exalted above change and annihilation because those are signs of contingency (hadath) that characterize the created world. The argument is that if a being can change from one state to another, it shows that the first state was not essential to it; otherwise, it could not leave that state. Similarly, an essence that is not self-subsisting but accepts accidents and changes is temporal. Allah, being eternal, does not change. The conclusion is that Allah is not subject to temporal attributes.

Key Terms
مكان
تغير
زوال
حدث
ذات
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that since Allah is described as 'above the throne,' He is literally in a place or on a physical throne.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that Allah was without place before creation and remains so; hence He is not confined to any place, including the throne. The 'above' is a metaphor for sovereignty.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do change and transition of states indicate about a being?
A) Eternity
B) Perfection
C) Temporality and contingency
D) Divine power

Key Takeaway
Allah's existence is independent of place and time; He does not change because change is a sign of contingency, and the Eternal is above contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is without place, does your heart truly feel that He is closer to you than your jugular vein, or do you unconsciously limit His presence to a specific location—like the mosque or your prayer rug? What can you do today to cultivate the certainty that He is always with you, no matter where you are?
?
Maturidi says Allah was as He is now. When you look at your own life, you change constantly—moods, beliefs, commitments. Does the thought that Allah never changes give you security or discomfort? Which areas of your life do you need to align with His unchanging commands, and what is holding you back?
?
If change is a sign of temporality, and you are a being that changes every moment, how does that remind you of your own contingency? Are you living with the urgent awareness that your existence depends on the Changeless One every second, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized this dependence?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Sign Objection
Maturidi argues from Musa's and Ibrahim's stories to affirm ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that Musa's request to see Allah was not a request for a sign, but rather a request for vision itself, and how this supports the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Musa's request was for vision, not a sign, as shown by Allah's response and Ibrahim's argument.

Some have argued that when Musa asked to see Allah, he was merely asking for a sign to confirm divine presence. Maturidi refutes this by showing that the Quranic text and prophetic precedent point to a literal request for vision. In this passage, he dismantles the sign objection with three clear proofs and strengthens his case with the example of Ibrahim's debate with his people.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get an overall sense of the argument.
2
Identify Maturidi's main claim
Notice that Maturidi begins by stating that if vision were impossible, Musa's request would imply ignorance, which is incompatible with prophethood.
This is a logical 'reductio ad absurdum'.
3
Examine the three refutations
Maturidi gives three reasons why Musa's request cannot be for a sign: (a) Allah said 'You will never see Me' yet showed a sign; (b) asking for signs is stubbornness; (c) the verse about the mountain settling distinguishes sign from vision.
Each reason is self-contained.
4
Understand the Ibrahim argument
Finally, Maturidi cites Ibrahim's argument with his people. Ibrahim did not reject a Lord who can be seen, but a Lord who sets, indicating that visibility is not a flaw.
This supports the permissibility of ru'ya.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this passage defends the Sunni doctrine that Allah will be seen by believers in the afterlife, against those who deny it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجوز الرؤية لكان ذلك السؤال منه جهلاً بربه، ومن يجهله لا يُحتمل أن يكون موضعا لرسالته، أمينا على وحيه. وبعد، فإن الله تعالى لم ينهه ولا أيأسه وبدون ذلك نهى نوحاً وعاتب آدم، وغيرهما من الرسل. وذلك لو كان لا يجوز يبلغ الكفر. ثم قال: فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني [الأعراف، ١٤٣/٧]. فإن قيل: لعله سأل آية يعلم بها ربه. قيل: لا يحتمل ذا لوجوه. (أ) أحدها أنه قال: لن تراني؛ وقد أراه الآية. (ب) وأيضا إن طلب الآيات يخرج مخرج التعنت، وقد أراه الآيات. وذلك تعنت الكفرة: إنهم لا يزالون يطلبون الآيات وإن كانت الكفاية قد ثبتت، فمثله ذلك. (ج) وأيضا إنه قال: ﴿فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَاني﴾؛ والآية التي يستقر معها الجبل دون الآية التي لا يستقر معها؛ ثبت أنه لم يُرد بذلك الآية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا محاجة إبراهيم قومه في النجوم، وما ذكر بالأفول والغيبة؛ ولم يحاجهم بأن لا يحب ربًا يُرى، ولكن حاجهم بأن لا يحب ربا يأفل، إذ هو دليل عدم الدوام. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If vision were permissible, that question would be ignorance of his Lord, and one who is ignorant of Him cannot be fit for His message, trustworthy for His revelation. And moreover, Allah did not forbid him nor despair him, and without that He forbade Nuh and reproached Adam and other messengers. For if it were not permissible, it would reach disbelief. Then He said: 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me' [Al-A'raf 7:143]. If it is said: Perhaps he asked for a sign by which to know his Lord. It is said: This is not possible for reasons: (a) One: He said 'You will never see Me'; and He showed him the sign. (b) Also, asking for signs comes out as stubbornness, and He had shown him signs. That is the stubbornness of the disbelievers: they keep asking for signs even when sufficiency is established, so his case is similar. (c) Also, He said 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me'; the sign that the mountain would settle with is not the sign that it would not settle with; it is established that He did not intend the sign by that. And there is no power except with Allah. Also, Ibrahim's argument with his people about the stars, and what was mentioned of setting and absence; he did not argue with them that he does not love a Lord who is seen, but he argued with them that he does not love a Lord who sets, for that is evidence of impermanence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that Musa’s request to see Allah was actually a request for a sign. Maturidi begins by stating that if seeing Allah were impossible, then Musa’s question would be ignorance of his Lord, and an ignorant person cannot be a prophet. He then notes that Allah did not forbid or despair Musa, unlike other prophets who were reproached. He then refutes the claim that Musa asked for a sign with three points: first, Musa said 'You will never see Me' but Allah showed him a sign; second, asking for signs is characteristic of stubborn disbelievers, not prophets; third, the verse about the mountain settling distinguishes between the sign and the vision. Then Maturidi cites Ibrahim’s argument: Ibrahim did not reject a Lord who can be seen, but rejected a Lord who sets, because setting indicates change and impermanence. This supports that vision is not incompatible with divinity.

Key Terms
رؤية
آية
تعنت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa's request to see Allah was actually a request for a sign to confirm divine existence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that Musa's request was for the vision of Allah Himself, as evidenced by the Quranic response and the three refutations, and that such a request does not imply ignorance.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that Musa's request to see Allah was not a request for a sign?
A) Because Musa said 'You will never see Me' yet was shown a sign
B) Because signs are only for disbelievers
C) Because the mountain would not settle for a sign
D) Because Allah forbade seeking signs

Key Takeaway
Musa's request was for the vision of Allah, not a sign, as confirmed by the Quranic text and prophetic precedent, thereby affirming the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you long for Allah, do you seek signs and proofs from Him, or do you truly desire to see His face? What holds you back from that longing?
?
Musa asked to see Allah despite knowing His transcendence. Do you have the same audacity in your prayer, asking for His presence in your life, or do you settle for distant knowing?
?
Ibrahim rejected a Lord that 'sets.' What 'setting' things in your life—wealth, status, relationships—do you mistakenly rely on as if they were divine? Will you let them go to seek the Unchanging One?
