Dualists and Naturalists: The Problem of Multiple Creators
Maturidi refutes dualist and naturalist claims by showing they lack distinguishing signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dualists and naturalists cannot provide a distinguishing sign for their claimed creators.

Core Idea
Without a distinguishing sign, claims of multiple creators remain baseless.

Maturidi begins by addressing the claims of dualists and naturalists who posit multiple eternal principles. He argues that the world could be attributed to either God or creation, but these groups fail to provide any distinctive mark for their gods' acts. This lesson examines that failure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups
Read the passage to identify the dualists (zanadiqa) and naturalists/astrologers (ashab al-taba'i' wal-nujum) and their claims.
The dualists claim two eternal principles, the naturalists more than two.
2
Understand the key argument
Maturidi points out that God did not establish separate proofs for each of His acts, nor did He distinguish between accidents attributable to Him and those attributable to others. This makes it impossible to uniquely identify any creator's work.
The lack of a distinguishing mark (fasi) is central.
3
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The verse (23:91) argues that if there were multiple gods, each would take away what he created. Maturidi notes that on the dualists' view, God also did not take away His creation because He placed no mark on it.
This strengthens the case against multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالخلق؛ فكان العالم جملة يحتمل الكون بالله وبالخلق . فكان العالم جملة بعدد، وذلك قول الزنادقة في اثنين، وأصحاب الطبائع والنجوم في أكثر من اثنين.
وبعد، فإن الله جل ثناؤه لم يقم على قولهم حجة إحداثه سوى العالم، ولا حجة قدمه. ثم لم يفصل بين الأعراض التي هي صنع غيره وبين التي هي صنعه؛ لأن الاجتماع والزوال ونحو ذلك قد يوجد في العيان ولا يُرى له الجامع المحرك ; ويُحتمل أن يكون ذلك لغيره لا له وإن لم يعاين، إذ هو نظير ما يعاين منه. فصار ذلك كقول الثنوية وأصحاب الطبائع : تكون الأشياء بها من غير أن أقام كل منها دليل صنعه بما يُفصل به من صنع غيره؛ وذلك آية العجز . والمعنى الذي احتج الله به على كون العالم بكليته به أن قال : إذا لذهب كل إله بما خلق [المؤمنون، ۹۱/۲۳]؛ فعلى قولهم فالله تعالى أيضاً لم يذهب بما خلق، لأنه لم يجعل عليه علما.
وبعد، فإن الأجسام اللطيفة إذا توهمت تفرقها أجزاءا مما لا يتجزأ كان كل جزء منها امتنع ذلك عن الإدراك بالحس ليؤدي إلى العقل ; وقد يتهيأ جمعها بغير الله على قدر لطف الجواهر وكثافتها; فصارت حجج الله على درك الأجسام فعل غيره في الإمكان. ولم يُبيّن الله تعالى الخلق ما يعلمون به أنه منه بدليل يدفع الإمكان من غيره، ليقرر كونه منه فيما أحسهم، فكيف فيما غيب عنهم ? فضاهأوا به من ذكرتُ من الثنوية وغيرهم أنه لم يجعل أحد منهم على فعله دليلاً; إذ ما من شر إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك خيرا لأحد ; وكذا في الجواهر من الحرارة والبرودة إلى آخر ما تنتهي إليه الطبائع ، وكذلك النجوم السيارة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And with creation; thus the world as a whole is susceptible to existence by God and by creation. Thus the world as a whole is by number, and that is the saying of the zanadiqa in two, and the adherents of natures and the stars in more than two. Afterwards, God, exalted is His praise, did not establish for their saying a proof of His originating creation other than the world, nor a proof of His eternity. Then He did not distinguish between the accidents that are the making of other than Him and those that are His making; because conjunction and separation and the like may exist in direct observation, and the one who brings together and moves is not seen for it; and it is possible that that is from other than Him, not from Him, even if not witnessed, since it is analogous to what is witnessed from Him. So that became like the saying of the dualists and the adherents of natures: things come to be by them without that any of them established a proof of His making by what distinguishes His making from the making of other than Him; and that is a sign of incapacity. And the meaning by which God argued for the world being entirely from Him is that He said: 'If there were other gods, each would have taken away what he created' [Quran 23:91]; so according to their saying, God also did not take away what He created, because He did not place upon it a mark. Afterwards, if the subtle bodies are imagined to be divided into parts that are indivisible, each part of them is concealed from perception by the senses to lead to the intellect; and their combination may be possible by other than God according to the subtlety and density of the substances; so God's arguments for the perception of bodies became the act of other than Him in possibility. And God did not make clear to creation what they know by which it is from Him with a proof that repels possibility from other than Him, to establish that it is from Him in what they perceive by sense, so how about what is hidden from them? So they resembled, with what I mentioned of the dualists and others, that not one of them placed upon his act a proof; for there is no evil but that it is possible for that to be good for someone; and likewise in the substances of heat and cold to the end of what the natures amount to, and likewise the wandering stars. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument against dualists (those who posit two eternal principles) and naturalists (who posit eternal elements like heat, cold, etc.) and astrologers. Maturidi begins by noting that the world could be attributed either to God or to creation; the dualists and naturalists claim multiple causes. He then argues that God did not provide a separate proof for each of His acts; rather, the world itself is the sign. The problem with the false claims is that they cannot distinguish between their alleged creators' acts and those of others. For example, the conjunction and separation of bodies are observed but the agent is not seen, so it could be attributed to anyone. If the dualists claim that things come to be from their gods, they cannot provide a distinguishing mark (فصل) that sets their gods' acts apart from others. Maturidi then invokes the Quranic verse (23:91) which argues that if there were multiple gods, each would take his creation away. Yet the dualists' gods do not take away their creations because no mark is placed on them. He then discusses subtle bodies and their parts; their combination could be due to other than God, so the divine proofs for the perception of bodies become equated with the possibility of others' acts. Ultimately, the dualists and naturalists resemble each other in failing to provide a clear proof for their claims; anything can be reinterpreted as good or evil depending on perspective. The passage concludes by affirming that power is only with God.

Key Terms
ثنوية (dualists)
طبائع (natures/elements)
حجة (proof)
فصل (distinguishing sign)
أعراض (accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple creators can coexist if they share creation harmoniously, but Maturidi argues that without distinguishing marks, their claims are indistinguishable and thus baseless.
Correction: The lack of a distinguishing sign (fasi) shows that the dualists and naturalists cannot prove that their claimed creators are responsible for specific phenomena, rendering their position rationally indefensible.

Checkpoint
What does the Quranic verse (23:91) argue according to Maturidi?
A) God alone creates everything
B) If there were other gods, each would take away what he created
C) The world is eternal
D) Humans cannot perceive divine acts

Key Takeaway
Dualists and naturalists cannot provide a distinguishing sign for their claimed creators, making their multiple-god theories rationally unsupported.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes attribute your successes to multiple sources (your effort, luck, circumstances) without acknowledging the single, sovereign hand of Allah? How does this passage on distinguishing signs challenge you to give Him alone the credit?
?
If you were to write a 'distinguishing mark' on your heart that proves your faith is from the one true Creator, would it be visible to others? What sign would they see?
?
The dualists and naturalists could not provide evidence for their multiple gods. Are there any 'gods' in your life—things you treat as ultimate authorities or sources—that you have not critically examined for proof? Will you dare to ask them for their distinguishing sign today?

The Argument from Subtle Bodies and the Failure of Proof
Maturidi uses the divisibility and combination of subtle bodies to show the insufficiency of dualist/naturalist proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the argument from subtle bodies to further refute dualists and naturalists by showing that divine proofs become indistinguishable from natural possibility.

Core Idea
If subtle bodies can be combined by other than God, the supposed divine proofs lose their exclusivity.

Maturidi continues his critique by considering subtle bodies (al-ajsam al-latifa). If these can be divided into indivisible parts and conceivably combined by causes other than God, then the arguments God gives for perceiving bodies become equivalent to the possibility of other agents. This lesson explores that argument and its conclusion that false claimants cannot provide any proof for their gods.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of subtle bodies
Maturidi posits that subtle bodies, if divided into smallest parts, become imperceptible to the senses and lead to intellectual reasoning.
These parts are indivisible (mimma la yatajaza).
2
Recognize the possibility of alternative causation
Their combination can be achieved by other than God, depending on the subtlety or density of substances. This makes God's proofs for perceiving bodies seem like the act of another.
Possibility (imkan) is key here.
3
Apply the reasoning to the overall argument
Since God did not provide a clear proof to rule out possibility of other causes, the dualists and naturalists are in the same position: they cannot offer a definitive sign for their acts.
This ties back to the earlier failure to provide distinguishing marks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: The fact that bodies can be divided and combined by other agents shows that sense perception alone does not guarantee divine causation; distinguishing proofs require more.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens when subtle bodies are divided into indivisible parts?
A) They become visible to the eye
B) They become imperceptible to the senses and lead to the intellect
C) They prove the existence of multiple gods
D) They become eternal

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world and see its order and complexity, do you ever assume your understanding of why things happen is complete? Maturidi argues that even clear natural phenomena could be caused by something other than God. How does this unsettle your own certainty about the proofs you rely on for faith? Will you let this push you to seek God's guidance rather than trust your own reasoning?
?
The text says 'there is no evil but that it is possible for that to be good for someone.' Think of the hardest trial you have faced. Have you truly considered that it might be a hidden good from God, or have you judged it as purely negative? Are you willing to remain open to the possibility that your worst moments may be part of a wisdom you cannot yet see? What would it cost you to accept that?
?
Maturidi states that God did not provide a proof that eliminates the possibility of other causes. Do you find this frustrating? Do you wish God had made His signs absolutely unambiguous? Or does this lack of absolute proof actually reveal something about how He wants you to relate to Him—with trust, not just logical certainty? How will you respond to the discomfort of living with that uncertainty?

Two Logical Arguments Refuting Dualism
Maturidi presents two logical arguments refuting the dualist claim of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical arguments used by early Muslim theologians to refute the claim that there are two eternal gods.

Core Idea
Two eternal gods cannot both be divine because they would either be ignorant or incapable.

Imam al-Maturidi presents two concise but powerful arguments against the dualist claim. These arguments are purely rational, relying on the logical implications of having two all-powerful beings. The first argument shows that dualism leads to ignorance or incapacity; the second shows that contradictory wills cannot coexist. Both arguments point to the necessity of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own wording set the stage.
2
Identify the two arguments
Look for the two 'حرفين' (points). The first starts with 'أحدهما', the second with 'والثاني'.
3
Understand the dilemma in the first argument
Each god can conceal things from the other. If yes → ignorance; if no → incapacity. Both negate divinity.
Follow the conditional: 'if they say yes... if they say no...'
4
Reflect on the second argument
The two gods would have contradictory wills: one wants to affirm, the other to negate. This leads to contradiction.
Contradiction cannot coexist in a coherent reality.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The orderly existence of the world indicates that there is only one God, not two.
The conclusion is: 'فدل الوجود على كون الواحد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفي قول الثنوية بحرفين. أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منهما على أن يُسر شيئًا عن الآخر ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر. حتى إذا قالوا: نعم، جهلوهما أو أحدهما وإن قالوا: لا ، عَجزوهما، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية. والثاني، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، فيتناقض. فدل الوجود على كون الواحد.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The people of tawhid argued for the negation of the dualists' statement with two points. The first is the ability of each one of them to conceal something from the other and to be able to do what the other does not know. Such that if they say: yes, they make both or one of them ignorant; and if they say: no, they render them incapable; and ignorance and incapacity nullify lordship. The second is that what this one wants to establish, the other wants to negate, so they contradict each other. So existence indicates the oneness (of God).
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a proof (استدلال) by the people of tawhid (the monotheists) against the dualists (those who claim two eternal gods, e.g., good and evil). Maturidi presents two arguments (حرفين). The first: each of the two alleged gods must have the ability to conceal something from the other and to do things unknown to the other. The dualists are then faced with a dilemma: if they affirm that each can hide things, then both gods are ignorant of something, or one is ignorant. If they deny this ability, then the gods are incapable. Both ignorance and incapacity are incompatible with divinity (ربوبية). The second argument: the two gods would have contradictory wills—one wants to affirm something, the other to negate it—leading to contradiction. The existence of the world as a coherent whole indicates that there is only one God.

Key Terms
توحيد (tawhid/monotheism)
ثنوية (dualists)
ربوبية (lordship)
حرف (point/argument)
جهل (ignorance)
عجز (incapacity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument relies on assuming the dualists' gods are personal and have human-like psychology.
Correction: Maturidi’s argument is purely logical and applies to any two alleged deities, regardless of their nature.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument (الحرف الأول) used against the dualists?
A) The two gods would create the same world
B) Each god can hide something from the other, leading to ignorance or incapacity
C) The two gods would love each other
D) The two gods would have the same nature

Key Takeaway
The two logical arguments demonstrate that the existence of two eternal gods is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second argument shows that two gods would have contradictory wills. Reflect on your own life: are there areas where you are trying to serve two 'gods'—like wealth and God—and the contradiction is causing inner conflict? Will you commit today to sorting out your priorities?
?
The first argument reveals that ignorance and incapacity nullify divinity. Have you ever implicitly attributed knowledge or power to anyone besides God in a way that diminished your tawhid? What one concrete step can you take today to realign your belief?
?
The source concludes: 'Existence indicates the oneness of God.' Do you consciously see the unity of creation as evidence of a single Creator in your daily observations? How can you train yourself to notice His signs more deeply starting today?

First Proof: The Dilemma of Ignorance or Incapacity
Maturidi presents the first logical dilemma against dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first logical argument against dualism that forces the dualist to admit either ignorance or incapacity in one of the two supposed gods.

Core Idea
Two gods would imply one is ignorant or weak, negating divinity.

Imam Maturidi begins by presenting a powerful dilemma. If two gods exist, each must have full power and knowledge. The dualist cannot escape the conclusion that one god would either be ignorant of the other's actions or unable to act if the other hides something. Both outcomes eliminate any claim to lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if each can hide, then ignorance; if not, then incapacity.'
The word 'يُسر' means 'to hide' or 'to conceal'.
2
Understand the dilemma
The dualist must choose: either each god can hide things from the other (leading to ignorance) or not (leading to incapacity). Both options contradict the perfection required for lordship.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ignorance and incapacity negate lordship (rububiyya). Therefore, the existence of two gods is impossible.
This argument does not assume that a god actually hides; it uses a hypothetical to expose an absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفي قول الثنوية بحرفين. أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منهما على أن يُسر شيئًا عن الآخر ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر. حتى إذا قالوا: نعم، جهلوهما أو أحدهما وإن قالوا: لا ، عَجزوهما، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية. والثاني، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، فيتناقض. فدل الوجود على كون الواحد.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The people of tawhid argued for negating the statement of the dualists by two points. One of them: by the ability of each one of them to hide something from the other and to be able to do what the other does not know. Until when they say: 'Yes,' they have made them ignorant or one of them ignorant; and if they say: 'No,' they have rendered them incapable. Ignorance and incapacity negate lordship. And the second: that what this one wants to affirm, the other wants to negate, so they contradict. So existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two logical proofs against the dualist belief in two gods. It begins by identifying the passage as a refutation of dualists. The first proof argues that if two gods exist, each must have power (qudra) over his own actions and knowledge of the other's actions. If one can hide something from the other or act without the other's knowledge, then the other is either ignorant (jahl) or incapable (ʿajz). Both ignorance and incapacity negate lordship (rububiyya). The dualist must either admit ignorance/capability or deny the ability to hide; both lead to the negation of divinity. The second proof points out that if two gods exist, their wills would contradict each other—one wants to affirm something, the other wants to negate it—leading to a contradiction (tanaquḍ). Thus, the existence of the world indicates the existence of a single God (al-Wahid).

Key Terms
ثنوية
قدرة
جهل
عجز
ربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that a god can actually hide something from another god.
Correction: The argument is a conditional dilemma: it forces the dualist to accept either ignorance or incapacity regardless of whether hiding occurs. It does not assert that hiding is actual.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof conclude if the dualist says one god can hide from the other?
A) That god is incapable.
B) That god is ignorant.
C) That both gods are perfect.
D) That the hiding proves dualism.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that any two-god hypothesis leads to at least one god being ignorant or weak, which contradicts the concept of lordship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the lesson, the people of tawhid used rational proof to defend the oneness of Allah. Have you ever, even silently in your heart, attributed to Allah a quality of ignorance or weakness in your understanding of His decree or knowledge of your private affairs? What thought or feeling prompted that, and how will you use the knowledge of this proof to purify your conviction starting today?
?
This proof forces the dualist into an impossible dilemma. If you were to present this argument to someone who doubts the oneness of Allah, are you confident enough to explain it clearly? What specific step will you take this week to internalize this reasoning so that you can defend tawhid with clarity and conviction?

Second Proof: The Contradiction of Wills
Maturidi presents the second proof from contradictory wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second logical argument against dualism: that two gods would have contradictory wills, making coherent existence impossible.

Core Idea
Two gods would always contradict each other's will.

The second proof is even more straightforward. If two gods exist, they would have independent wills. What one wants to bring into existence, the other may want to negate. This leads to a direct contradiction, which cannot occur in reality. Therefore, existence points to a single God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic for the second proof and its translation. Note the stark simplicity: 'what this one wants to affirm, the other wants to negate.'
2
Grasp the contradiction
If one god wills X and the other wills not-X, then both cannot be actualized. Since the world exists coherently, such contradiction cannot be present in the divine source.
3
Connect to tawhid
The existence of a coherent universe implies a single will behind it. This argument reinforces the core Islamic belief in divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : واستدل أهل التوحيد على نفي قول الثنوية بحرفين. أحدهما بقدرة كل واحد منهما على أن يُسر شيئًا عن الآخر ويقدر أن يفعل ما لا يعلمه الآخر. حتى إذا قالوا: نعم، جهلوهما أو أحدهما وإن قالوا: لا ، عَجزوهما، والجهل والعجز يسقطان الربوبية. والثاني، أن ما أراد هذا إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، فيتناقض. فدل الوجود على كون الواحد.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The people of tawhid argued for negating the statement of the dualists by two points. One of them: by the ability of each one of them to hide something from the other and to be able to do what the other does not know. Until when they say: 'Yes,' they have made them ignorant or one of them ignorant; and if they say: 'No,' they have rendered them incapable. Ignorance and incapacity negate lordship. And the second: that what this one wants to affirm, the other wants to negate, so they contradict. So existence indicates the existence of the One.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two logical proofs against the dualist belief in two gods. It begins by identifying the passage as a refutation of dualists. The first proof argues that if two gods exist, each must have power (qudra) over his own actions and knowledge of the other's actions. If one can hide something from the other or act without the other's knowledge, then the other is either ignorant (jahl) or incapable (ʿajz). Both ignorance and incapacity negate lordship (rububiyya). The dualist must either admit ignorance/capability or deny the ability to hide; both lead to the negation of divinity. The second proof points out that if two gods exist, their wills would contradict each other—one wants to affirm something, the other wants to negate it—leading to a contradiction (tanaquḍ). Thus, the existence of the world indicates the existence of a single God (al-Wahid).

Key Terms
تناقض
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that two gods would necessarily disagree on everything.
Correction: The argument only requires that it is possible for them to disagree on at least one thing; the possibility of contradiction is enough to show the absurdity, because a perfect being cannot be subject to potential conflict.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude from the possibility of contradictory wills?
A) That two gods can coexist if they cooperate.
B) That existence indicates a single God.
C) That the world is an illusion.
D) That the first god is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that two gods would inevitably have opposing wills, which contradicts the orderly existence of the universe, affirming the necessity of one God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If two independent wills cannot coexist without contradiction, what does that reveal about the divided loyalties in your own heart? Are you trying to serve multiple masters—your desires, people’s approval, and Allah? How does this proof call you to single-hearted devotion to the One whose will alone sustains existence?
?
Consider your own life: do you find yourself affirming something in faith but negating it by your actions? How does the logical impossibility of contradiction in God's will expose the incoherence of a life that claims tawhid but behaves as if multiple wills have authority? What must you unify starting today?
?
The proof shows that existence itself would be impossible with multiple gods. Is your existence coherent, or is it torn by contradictory commitments—e.g., professing Islam while living by secular values? What would it mean for you to let the unity of God (الواحد) become the single organizing principle of your entire life?

Maturidi's Cosmological Argument
Maturidi argues from the world's neediness and order to a creator with knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's cosmological argument from the world's neediness and order to the existence of a creator with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The world's inability to manage itself proves it needs a knowing, powerful creator.

Imam Maturidi begins his argument by observing the world around us. Everything we see is constrained, incapable, and full of opposites. From these obvious facts, reason concludes that the world could not have come about by chance or by itself. Maturidi then argues that the only explanation is a creator with knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Observe the world's characteristics
Read the passage and note the attributes Maturidi gives to the world: constrained, incapable of self-management, ignorant of its beginning, containing opposites.
These observations are the starting point of the argument.
2
Understand the conclusion of reason
See how reason infers two things: the world did not come to be by itself, and its arranger must have knowledge and power.
The argument is based on the principle that effects require causes.
3
Reflect on the exclusion of chance
Maturidi explicitly denies that the world came about by chance or by its own power. Why does he think this is important?
Chance cannot produce consistent order.
4
Connect to the witnessed world (al-shāhid)
Learn why Maturidi says the only way to know the unseen cause is by analogy with the witnessed world.
This methodology is key in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنا لما وجدنا جميع ما يعاين من العالم مضطراً عاجزاً عن تدبير نفسه، جاهلاً ببدء حاله وبمقدار الأخذ في كل أحوال من الزمان والمكان [الذي فيه يتقلب وبه يكون مجتمعا، فيه الأضداد التي هي بحق الطباع متنافرة، عقل أنه لا كان بنفسه وعقل أن الذي دبّره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة. إذ خرج على غير احتمال الاتفاق لذاته، و[خرج] لا على دلالة قوة له بنفسه، وعلم بحاله . فلا بد من تحقيق المعنى الذي في الشاهد دليله؛ إذ لا وجه لمعرفته إلا به. وكذلك لو كان ذلك بتدبير من دونه فإليه يرجع الأمر الأول ، وفي ذلك كله ما ذكرنا .
Afterwards, since we find everything that is observed of the world—constrained, incapable of managing itself, ignorant of its beginning state and of the measure of taking in every state of time and place in which it changes and by which it is composed, containing opposites which by nature are mutually repugnant—reason concludes that it did not come to be by itself, and reason concludes that the one who arranged it and determined it had knowledge of it and power over it. For it came forth not by way of chance for its essence, and it came forth not by indication of a power in itself, and its state is known. So there must be a verification of the meaning which in the witnessed world is its proof; for there is no way to know it except by that. And likewise, if that [arrangement] were by the management of another, then to Him [the first] the first matter returns. And in all that is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents a cosmological argument for God's existence and attributes. Maturidi observes that everything in the world is constrained, incapable of self-management, ignorant of its origin and future, and composed of opposites that naturally repel each other. From this, reason concludes that the world did not arise by itself and that its arranger and determiner must have knowledge and power. The argument proceeds: the world's existence is not due to chance nor to any inherent power; therefore, it must be the act of a knowing, powerful agent. This understanding must be based on analogy with the witnessed world (al-shāhid), as that is the only way to know such things. If one posits an intermediate manager, the ultimate cause still returns to the first.

Key Terms
عالم
تدبير
اتفاق
قدرة
علم
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could be self-sufficient or come about by chance.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the world's deficiencies prove it cannot be self-sufficient or random; it requires a creator with knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the opposites in the world, which are mutually repugnant by nature, indicate?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world was created by chance.
C) The world cannot be self-originating.
D) The world has no creator.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's cosmological argument demonstrates that the world's neediness and order point necessarily to a creator who is knowing and powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness the world's order every day—your own heartbeat, the changing seasons, the complexity of a single cell. Do you truly believe in your core that none of this could have come from itself, or do you live as if it is self-sufficient? What would change if you reminded yourself daily of its utter neediness?
?
Maturidi says the world is 'ignorant of its beginning state and of the measure of taking in every state of time and place.' You, unlike the world, have been given knowledge. Do you use that knowledge to see the Creator, or do you let it make you proud? When you look at the universe, does it humble you into worship or inflate your sense of intellectual independence?
?
If the world's very existence is a proof of a knowing and powerful Creator, then your own existence is a walking sign. Are you living as that sign—pointing to Him with your words, choices, and character? Or have you become a 'sign' that is read carelessly, as if your life were random?

Classification of Entities: Simple and Composite
Maturidi classifies entities into simple and composite types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of fundamental entities into simple pillars (accidents and attributes) and composite substances (bodies).

Core Idea
The world's building blocks are either simple attributes or composite bodies.

Maturidi begins by establishing the basic categories of existence. He divides all created entities into two types: the simple pillars (الأركان البسيطة) which are accidents and attributes, and the composite substances (الأعيان المركبة) which are bodies. This classification is foundational for his later proofs, providing the vocabulary needed to discuss the world's dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification statement
Read the Arabic sentence that divides entities into two categories. Notice the terminology: 'simple pillars' and 'composite substances'.
The phrase 'وبالله المعونة' is a pious invocation, not part of the classification.
2
Identify the two categories
Identify 'simple pillars' as accidents and attributes, and 'composite substances' as bodies. Reflect on what each term means in Islamic philosophy.
Accidents are non-substantial properties; attributes here refer to qualities inhering in a subject.
3
Understand 'simple pillars' in detail
Consider why accidents and attributes are grouped together as 'simple'. They are indivisible and dependent on a substance for their existence.
4
Understand 'composite substances'
Bodies are composite because they are formed from atoms or parts. They are the primary subjects in which accidents inhere.
5
Reflect on the purpose of this classification
Think about why Maturidi starts with this classification. It sets the stage for his argument that the world, composed of these entities, is in need of a creator.
This classification is a tool for the proof that follows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأركان البسيطة وهي الأعراض والصفات، والأعيان المركبة وهي الأجسام. وبالله المعونة. وبعد، فإنا لما وجدنا جميع ما يعاين من العالم مضطرًا عاجزاً عن تدبير نفسه، جاهلاً ببدء حاله وبمقدار الأخذ في كل أحوال من الزمان والمكان الذي فيه يتقلب وبه يكون مجتمعا، فيه الأضداد التي هي بحق الطباع متنافرة، عقل أنه لا كان بنفسه وعقل أن الذي دبّره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة. إذ خرج على غير احتمال الاتفاق لذاته، وخرج لا على دلالة قوة له بنفسه، وعلم بحاله . فلا بد من تحقيق المعنى الذي في الشاهد دليله؛ إذ لا وجه لمعرفته إلا به. وكذلك لو كان ذلك بتدبير من دونه فإليه يرجع الأمر الأول، وفي ذلك كله ما ذكرنا.
The simple pillars are the accidents and attributes, and the composite substances are the bodies. And with God is assistance. Afterwards, since we find all that is seen of the world to be compelled, incapable of managing itself, ignorant of the beginning of its state and of the measure of progress in every state of time and place in which it turns and by which it is assembled, containing opposites which by the right of natures are mutually repugnant, the intellect [concludes] that it did not come to be by itself, and the intellect [concludes] that the one who managed it and determined it had knowledge of it and power over it. For it came forth not on the possibility of coincidence for itself, and came forth not on an indication of a power in itself, and it is known by its state. So there must be verification of the meaning whose evidence is in the witnessed [world]; for there is no way to know it except by it. And likewise, if that were by the management of someone other than Him, then it returns to the first matter; and in all that is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a proof. First, Maturidi classifies the fundamental entities of the world: simple pillars (which are accidents and attributes) and composite substances (bodies). He then begins the proof by observing that everything in the world is compelled ( مضطرًا ) and incapable of managing itself, ignorant of its own beginning and of the course of change in time and place. The world contains mutually repelling opposites (like fire and water), which cannot naturally coexist. The intellect judges that such a world could not have originated by itself; it must have a manager who knows it and has power over it. The 'coming forth' (خرج) of the world is not due to chance (الاتفاق) nor due to any inherent power in itself. Therefore, we must verify the meaning that is evident in the witnessed world; there is no other way to know the Creator except through this evidence. Even if the world were managed by some intermediary, the chain ultimately goes back to the First Cause. This argument establishes that the Creator possesses both knowledge (علم) and power (قدرة).

Key Terms
الأركان البسيطة
الأعراض
الصفات
الأعيان المركبة
الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following are classified as 'simple pillars'?
A) Accidents and bodies
B) Accidents and attributes
C) Bodies and attributes
D) Bodies and substances

Key Takeaway
Maturidi classifies all created entities into two types: simple pillars (accidents and attributes) and composite substances (bodies).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Maturidi classifies all created entities into simple accidents/attributes and composite bodies. Consider your own body: it is composite, dependent on accidents. But what about your awareness of God? Is that an accident, a body, or something else? Have you ever considered that your very being is contingent on a Creator who is neither simple nor composite in the same way? Let this classification humble you: you are not self-sufficient. How will you acknowledge your dependence on God today?
?
Maturidi states 'And with God is assistance' at the end of this brief classification. This indicates that even understanding the basic structure of creation relies on divine aid. Think about your own study of these concepts: are you relying on your intellect alone, or are you actively seeking God’s assistance to grasp the truth? In what specific area of your understanding do you need to say 'Billaah al-mu'oonah' (with God is assistance) today?
?
If everything in the world is either a simple attribute or a composite body, then where does your soul fit? Does it belong to the category of simple pillars? Or is it something different? How does this classification challenge or affirm your belief in the afterlife? Reflect on the reality that your existence is not self-sustaining; you are made of parts and accidents that depend on God every moment. What change in your daily life would reflect this reliance?

Proof from Contingency: The World's Need for a Manager
Maturidi argues from the world's contingency to a creator with knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world's dependence, ignorance, and internal contradictions prove it requires a creator with knowledge and power.

Core Idea
The world's inability to manage itself proves it must have a knowing and powerful creator.

Having classified entities, Maturidi now presents a rational proof. He observes that everything in the world is compelled (مضطرًا), incapable of self-management, ignorant of its own beginning, and contains mutually repulsive opposites. The intellect concludes that such a world could not have arisen by itself or by chance; it must have a manager who possesses knowledge and power. This argument is a classic contingency argument in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'وبعد' to the end. Notice the premises Maturidi lists about the world's state.
The argument is a chain of observations leading to a rational conclusion.
2
Identify the premises
Maturidi lists: the world is compelled, incapable of self-management, ignorant of its own state, contains mutually repulsive opposites. Underline each premise.
3
Understand the conclusion
From these premises, the intellect judges that the world could not have come to be by itself or by chance. It must have a manager who has knowledge and power.
The terms 'knowledge' (علم) and 'power' (قدرة) are explicitly stated.
4
Note the role of opposites
The presence of naturally repulsive opposites (like fire and water) in a single world shows that their coexistence cannot be due to nature itself; it requires an external agent.
This is a key point in the argument.
5
Reflect on the method
Maturidi says the only way to know the Creator is through 'the witnessed world' (الشاهد). This is an empirical-rational approach.
He emphasizes that the evidence is in the world, not in abstract speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأركان البسيطة وهي الأعراض والصفات، والأعيان المركبة وهي الأجسام. وبالله المعونة. وبعد، فإنا لما وجدنا جميع ما يعاين من العالم مضطرًا عاجزاً عن تدبير نفسه، جاهلاً ببدء حاله وبمقدار الأخذ في كل أحوال من الزمان والمكان الذي فيه يتقلب وبه يكون مجتمعا، فيه الأضداد التي هي بحق الطباع متنافرة، عقل أنه لا كان بنفسه وعقل أن الذي دبّره وقدره كان له به علم وعليه قدرة. إذ خرج على غير احتمال الاتفاق لذاته، وخرج لا على دلالة قوة له بنفسه، وعلم بحاله . فلا بد من تحقيق المعنى الذي في الشاهد دليله؛ إذ لا وجه لمعرفته إلا به. وكذلك لو كان ذلك بتدبير من دونه فإليه يرجع الأمر الأول، وفي ذلك كله ما ذكرنا.
The simple pillars are the accidents and attributes, and the composite substances are the bodies. And with God is assistance. Afterwards, since we find all that is seen of the world to be compelled, incapable of managing itself, ignorant of the beginning of its state and of the measure of progress in every state of time and place in which it turns and by which it is assembled, containing opposites which by the right of natures are mutually repugnant, the intellect [concludes] that it did not come to be by itself, and the intellect [concludes] that the one who managed it and determined it had knowledge of it and power over it. For it came forth not on the possibility of coincidence for itself, and came forth not on an indication of a power in itself, and it is known by its state. So there must be verification of the meaning whose evidence is in the witnessed [world]; for there is no way to know it except by it. And likewise, if that were by the management of someone other than Him, then it returns to the first matter; and in all that is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a proof. First, Maturidi classifies the fundamental entities of the world: simple pillars (which are accidents and attributes) and composite substances (bodies). He then begins the proof by observing that everything in the world is compelled ( مضطرًا ) and incapable of managing itself, ignorant of its own beginning and of the course of change in time and place. The world contains mutually repelling opposites (like fire and water), which cannot naturally coexist. The intellect judges that such a world could not have originated by itself; it must have a manager who knows it and has power over it. The 'coming forth' (خرج) of the world is not due to chance (الاتفاق) nor due to any inherent power in itself. Therefore, we must verify the meaning that is evident in the witnessed world; there is no other way to know the Creator except through this evidence. Even if the world were managed by some intermediary, the chain ultimately goes back to the First Cause. This argument establishes that the Creator possesses both knowledge (علم) and power (قدرة).

Key Terms
مضطرًا
عاجز
جاهلاً
أضداد
تدبير
قدرة
علم
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's 'ignorance' means the world is literally unaware; the proof relies on a conscious entity being ignorant.
Correction: Maturidi means the world lacks knowledge of its own origin and management—it is not self-sufficient or self-explanatory, not that it is sentient.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the manager of the world from the argument?
A) The manager is the intellect itself
B) The manager must have knowledge and power
C) The manager is the world's own inherent force
D) The manager does not exist

Key Takeaway
The world's contingency and internal contradictions rationally demand a creator with knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every detail of your life—your strengths, weaknesses, and circumstances—is not accidental but deliberately managed by a Creator with perfect knowledge and power? How would your daily attitude change if you lived with this conviction?
?
The world is described as 'ignorant of its own beginning.' You, however, have been told your origin. In what ways do you still live as if you don't know where you came from or where you are going? What step will you take today to align your life with this truth?
?
This proof shows that the world is incapable of managing itself. In what area of your life are you still trying to be self-sufficient, refusing to rely on the One who manages all things? Will you surrender that area to Him today?

The Invalidity of Asking 'Why Did God Create?'
Introduction to the question and first opinion denying its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that the question 'Why did God create?' is invalid due to God's eternal attributes of wisdom, knowledge, and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
God's perfect attributes make His acts inherently wise, so asking 'why' is meaningless.

This lesson opens Imam Maturidi's discussion of why God created the universe. The first opinion presented argues that the question itself is invalid. By grounding the argument in God's attributes of wisdom, knowledge, and self-sufficiency, Maturidi sets the stage for a deeper theological inquiry into divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the opening praise and the immediate mention of differing opinions.
2
Identify the key attributes
The passage states that God is eternally wise (حكيم), knowing (عليم), and self-sufficient (غني). Reflect on how each attribute contributes to the argument.
3
Understand the reasoning
The argument claims that an act can only depart from wisdom due to ignorance or fear of losing benefit. Since God lacks both, His act cannot lack wisdom. Therefore, the question 'why' is invalid.
4
Observe the Quranic evidence
The passage cites Quran 21:16-23 to negate the notion of play and to affirm that God is not questioned. See how the verses support the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الذي لا غاية لما يستحق من الشكر والمحامد على ما لدينا من جزيل المنن وعظيم العوائد، وإياه نسأل التوفيق لأهدى سبل المراشد. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : اختلف الناس في جواب سؤال السائل: «لم خلق الله الخلق»؟ أ ـ قال قوم: السؤال فاسد لا يُسأل عن ذلك؛ إذ الله سبحانه حكيم لم يزل، عليم غنى؛ ففعله لا يحتمل الخروج عن الحكمة، إذ يخرج الفعل عنها لجهل بها، أو لما يُخاف فوت نفع لو حفظ طريق الحكمة. فإذا كان الله سبحانه عليمًا لا يجهل، غنيّاً لا تمسته حاجة ينتفع بدفعها بطل أن يخرج فعله عن جهة الحكمة وسؤال «لِمَ» ليست فيه الحكمة؛ ولذلك نفي توهم اللعب عن فعله فقال : وما خلقنا السماء والأرض وما بينهما لاعبين إلى قوله : ولا يسئل عما يفعل وهم يسئلون ، وألحق الويل بمن يظن به الحاجة أو في فعله السفة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, for whom there is no limit to what He deserves of thanks and praises for the abundant favors and great benefits we have. We ask Him for guidance to the most guided paths of right conduct. Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: People differed regarding the answer to the questioner's question: 'Why did Allah create creation?' A. Some people said: The question is invalid; one should not ask about that, because Allah, glorified be He, is eternally wise, knowing, self-sufficient. His act cannot depart from wisdom, for an act departs from it only due to ignorance of it, or for fear of losing a benefit if the way of wisdom were preserved. Since Allah is knowing, not ignorant, self-sufficient, not affected by any need whose removal would benefit Him, it is impossible that His act departs from the path of wisdom. The question 'why' does not involve wisdom; therefore He negated the notion of play from His act, saying: 'We did not create the heaven and the earth and what is between them in play' up to His saying: 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned.' And He attached woe to those who think He has need or that there is foolishness in His act. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on the question 'Why did God create creation?' with the basmala and praise. Imam Maturidi states that people have different answers. The first opinion (indicated by 'A') holds that the question itself is invalid. The reasoning is that God is eternally wise, knowledgeable, and self-sufficient. An act can only depart from wisdom due to ignorance (which God lacks) or fear of losing benefit (impossible for the self-sufficient). Therefore, God's act is always wise, and asking 'why' implies a lack of wisdom in the act. The opinion cites Quranic verses (21:16-23) where God denies creating in play and states He is not questioned. Attaching woe to those who impute need or foolishness to God reinforces the invalidity of the question.

Key Terms
حكمة
عليم
غني
لعب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the question 'why' is always valid and reasonable.
Correction: The opinion argues that due to God's perfect attributes, the question is invalid when applied to God because His acts are inherently wise and beyond questioning.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion, why is the question 'why did God create?' invalid?
A) Because the act is harmful
B) Because God is wise and self-sufficient
C) Because creation is unnecessary
D) Because the question is impossible to answer

Key Takeaway
The question 'Why did God create?' is invalid because God's eternal wisdom, knowledge, and self-sufficiency render His acts inherently wise and beyond questioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or a situation you don't understand, do you find yourself silently demanding that Allah explain Himself? How does this lesson challenge that impulse and call you to trust His perfect wisdom?
?
Is there a specific area of your life where you have questioned Allah's wisdom? What would it look like for you to surrender that question and affirm, 'He is not questioned about what He does'?
?
Reflect on the statement 'woe to those who think Allah has need or foolishness in His act.' Have you ever acted as though Allah 'needs' your obedience or worship? How does recognizing His absolute self-sufficiency reshape your worship into pure gratitude?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila on al-Aṣlaḥ (the Most Beneficial)
Maturidi deconstructs the Mu'tazila's claim of al-aṣlaḥ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila claim that God must act according to what is most beneficial, and how he shows it leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Claiming God must do the most beneficial reduces wisdom to foolishness.

The Mu'tazila argued that God, being all-wise, always does what is most beneficial for creation, so His actions are beyond question. Imam Maturidi subjects this claim to rigorous analysis, showing that if 'most beneficial' means wisdom, it is unproblematic but reduces to a prior view; if it means something else, the entire framework collapses into infinite regress and potential corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position and Maturidi's first response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila assert that God sees the most beneficial and acts accordingly, and that He is not asked about it.
Maturidi begins by stating that this claim must be examined carefully.
2
Understand the dilemma: al-aṣlaḥ as wisdom or something else
Maturidi argues that if al-aṣlaḥ means wisdom, it reduces to the first opinion already discussed. If it means something else, the problem of knowing al-aṣlaḥ is identical to asking 'why did He do?'—leading to infinite regress.
This is a powerful logical move: the very concept is either redundant or problematic.
3
Examine the critique that conditions for al-aṣlaḥ could be conditions for corruption
Maturidi points out that any condition set for al-aṣlaḥ could equally be a condition for corruption, and that would be the greatest corruption. Wisdom cannot become foolishness.
This undermines the Mu'tazila claim that al-aṣlaḥ is a necessary condition for God's actions.
4
Reflect on the nature of wisdom and justice
Maturidi defines wisdom as correctness and placing things in their proper place, which is justice. God's acts never depart from that. This establishes a consistent framework.
This positive definition contrasts with the relativistic notion of al-aṣlaḥ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب ـ وقال قوم من المعتزلة : رأى الأصلح كذلك ففعل، ولا يُسأل عن فعله الأصلح. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهذا كلام لا يخلو من أن يراد بالأصلح الحكمة، فهو الأول. وإن أراد به معنى سواه، فإن القول في معرفة الأصلح كهو في أنه «لِمَ فَعَلَ؟»، سواء. مع ما يُسأل عن شرط الأصلح له في الفعل : من أين يجب؟ على أن أحق الناس بالاستحياء من هذا اللفظ هم؛ إذ ليس من شيء يُجعل شرطا للأصلح إلا وأمكن أن يكون ذلك شرطا للفساد، ويكون به أعظم الفساد. ولا يجوز أن يكون شيء حكمة يصير سفها؛ لأن تأويل الأصلح أن يكون أصلح لغيره، وقد يكون به الفساد لآخر عندهم. وتأويل الحكمة الإصابة، وهو وضع كل شيء موضعه، وذلك معنى العدل، ولا يخرج فعله عن ذلك. وقال: إن الله خالق بذاته ، إذ هو اسم المدح والعظمة، ومحال أن يكون الله سبحانه يستحقه بغيره، لما فيه إيجاب النفع له ، ومن ذلك وصف فعله فهو محتاج . وإذ قد ثبت أنه خالق بذاته لم يجز أن لا يكون خالقا ألبتة، والسؤال عن الـ «لِمَ» محال كالسؤال عن «لم قدر ولم علم؟». ولا قوة إلا بالله .
B. Some of the Muʿtazila said: He saw the most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) likewise and so acted, and He is not asked about His most beneficial act. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This statement cannot be free from meaning by al-aṣlaḥ either wisdom, so it is the first [opinion]. If He intends by it a meaning other than wisdom, then the statement about knowing al-aṣlaḥ is like the statement about 'why He did?'—the same. Furthermore, He is asked about the condition of al-aṣlaḥ for Him in the act: from where is it obligatory? Indeed, the people most deserving of shame from this expression are they, because there is nothing made a condition for al-aṣlaḥ except that it could be a condition for corruption, and thereby be the greatest corruption. It is not permissible that something be wisdom and then become foolishness, because the interpretation of al-aṣlaḥ is that it is most beneficial for another, and it may cause corruption for another according to them. The interpretation of wisdom is correctness, placing everything in its proper place, and that is the meaning of justice, and His act does not depart from that. And He said: Allah is Creator by His essence, for it is a name of praise and majesty, and it is impossible that Allah, glorified be He, deserves it by another, because that would involve conferring benefit upon Him, and whoever describes His act as such is in need. And since it is established that He is Creator by His essence, it is not permissible that He be not Creator at all, and the question 'why' is impossible like the question 'why did He decree and know?' There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila opinion that God acts according to what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ) and thus cannot be asked 'why' as His act is already optimal. Imam Maturidi refutes this in detail. First, if al-aṣlaḥ means wisdom, then it reduces to the first opinion already discussed. If it means something else, then the problem of determining al-aṣlaḥ is identical to the original question 'why did He do?'—leading to infinite regress. Moreover, one can ask: from where does the obligation of al-aṣlaḥ come? The Mu'tazila are most deserving of shame because any condition for al-aṣlaḥ could just as easily be a condition for corruption, causing the greatest corruption. Wisdom cannot become foolishness; al-aṣlaḥ is relative to another and may cause corruption for someone else. True wisdom is placing things correctly, which is justice. The Shaykh then refutes the Mu'tazila view that God is Creator by His essence (which they use to avoid attributes), arguing that this implies need if His act is described as beneficial. Since God is Creator by essence, He must always create, and asking 'why' is like asking why He decreed or knew—impossible.

Key Terms
الأصلح (the most beneficial)
الحكمة (wisdom)
العدل (justice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God always does what is most beneficial for each individual creature in a human sense.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this notion is either redundant or leads to contradictions; true wisdom is placing everything in its proper order, which may involve what appears harmful to some.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main criticism of the Mu'tazila's concept of al-aṣlaḥ?
A) It is too difficult to determine
B) It reduces to wisdom or leads to infinite regress
C) It is against the Quran
D) It implies God is unjust

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila's al-aṣlaḥ argument is self-defeating: either it is redundant with wisdom or it leads to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems harmful or unfair, do you secretly expect Allah to give you what you think is 'most beneficial'? How does this lesson expose your assumption that you know what's truly best for you?
?
Have you ever accused Allah—even in your heart—of injustice because something you wanted was denied or something painful occurred? How does Maturidi's definition of wisdom as 'placing everything in its proper place' challenge your right to judge His actions?
?
The Mu'tazila made a condition on God based on their own reasoning. Do you make conditions on Allah in your duas or expectations? What would it take for you to surrender fully to His wisdom, even when it feels like corruption?

Allah as Creator by Essence and the Impossibility of Asking 'Why'
Maturidi argues Allah is Creator by essence, not by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah must be described as Creator by His own essence and why asking 'why' about His actions is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Allah is Creator by essence; asking why He creates is like asking why He knows.

Imam Maturidi concludes his refutation by addressing the Mu'tazila's attempts to avoid attributing actions to Allah's essence. He argues that Allah is described as Creator by His very essence—this is a name of praise—and therefore no question of 'why' can apply. The very attempt to ask 'why' about His creation implies need, which is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument that Allah is Creator by essence
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical progression: because 'Creator' is a name of praise and majesty, it must be essential, not acquired from another.
Maturidi uses this to refute the Mu'tazila who implicitly make Allah's essence dependent on His actions.
2
Understand why asking 'why' is impossible
If Allah is Creator by essence, then He is always Creator. Asking 'why did He create?' is like asking 'why did He decree?' or 'why did He know?'—these are attributes that cannot have a cause.
The question 'why' implies need or a purpose external to Allah, which is impossible.
3
Reflect on the implications for divine attributes
This argument establishes that Allah's creative act is a necessary consequence of His essence, not a contingent choice for some external benefit. It protects divine transcendence.
It also shows that the Mu'tazila's framing of al-aṣlaḥ would imply benefit for Allah, which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does Maturidi mean by 'Allah is Creator by essence'?

beginner
4 min
Creation as Necessary Due to Generosity
Opinion that God's generosity and power necessitate creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's attributes of generosity and power necessitate creation.

Core Idea
God's generosity and power require creation to be actualized.

This lesson explores an opinion that links God's attributes directly to the necessity of creation. The argument is simple: a generous being must bestow, and a powerful being's power must be actualized. This leads to the conclusion that creation is a necessary consequence, not an arbitrary act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: generous, noble, powerful.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two premises: God is generous and noble, and God is powerful. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why do these attributes necessitate creation? Because generosity requires bestowal, and power that never acts is wasted.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that creation exists to actualize God's attributes. Think about how this differs from other views.
This opinion is one of several discussed in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents a third opinion: God's attributes of generosity (jūd) and power (qudra) necessitate creation. The reasoning is that a generous being must bestow generosity, and a powerful being whose power never produces an act would have a wasted power. Therefore, God created in order to actualize His generosity and power. The opinion concludes by attributing success to Allah.

Invalidity of question due to preexistent cause
Opinion that asking 'why' implies an eternal cause besides God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why asking 'why' about God's creation can imply an eternal cause besides God, and how Maturidi's position avoids this problem.

Core Idea
Asking 'why did God create?' leads to an infinite regress or shirk.

Some theologians argued that the question 'why did God create?' is invalid because it necessitates a cause for creation. This cause either creates an endless chain or posits an eternal being other than God. Imam Maturidi counters that creation is by God's essence, not by a separate cause. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise argument structure.
The passage presents a compact logical dilemma.
2
Understand the dilemma about the cause
The argument says: If you ask 'why' about creation, you're implying a cause. That cause is either created (leading to infinite regress) or uncreated (making it another eternal being). Both are problematic.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Grasp Maturidi's resolution
Maturidi resolves the dilemma by stating that God creates by His essence, not through a separate cause. Thus the question 'why' is invalid because it presupposes a cause that doesn't exist.
This is consistent with Maturidi's emphasis on God's absolute uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د ـ وقال قوم: السؤال محال، لما يوجب تقدّم علة لما يخلق. والعلة إما أن تكون خلقاً ، فالسؤال عنها هو السؤال عن جملة ، أو لا تكون ، فتكون غير إله في الأزل . بل خلق بأن فعل الخلق بذاته على ما مر بيانه . والله الموفق.
D. Some people said: The question is impossible, because it requires a cause preceding what He creates. The cause is either created, so the question about it is the question about the whole, or it is not created, so it would be other than God in preeternity. Rather, He created by performing creation by His essence as has been explained. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This fourth opinion argues that asking 'why' is invalid because it implies a cause for creation that precedes it. If that cause is itself created, then the question applies to it as well, leading to an infinite regress. If it is not created, then it would be an eternal entity other than God, which is shirk. Therefore, the only sound position is that God creates by His essence without a separate cause, as previously explained. The opinion ends by acknowledging Allah as the grantor of success.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
أزل (preeternity)
خلق بذاته (creation by essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think there is always a reason (cause) for creation, so asking 'why' is legitimate.
Correction: This opinion shows that asking 'why' leads to problematic theological consequences: either infinite regress or positing an eternal cause other than God. Thus the question itself is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does this opinion identify as the problem with asking 'why did God create'?
A) It shows ignorance
B) It implies a cause that either creates infinite regress or associates partners with God
C) It is impossible to answer
D) It is a waste of time

Key Takeaway
Asking 'why' about creation leads to either an infinite regression of causes or shirk; Maturidi resolves this by asserting creation by God's essence without a separate cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face difficulty in understanding Allah's decree, do you find yourself silently demanding a reason? How will you train your heart to say 'He creates by His essence' even when your intellect cannot grasp the wisdom?
?
The argument shows that asking 'why' leads either to infinite regress or shirk. Have you ever, even unknowingly, attributed your success or failure to causes other than Allah's direct creation? This week, in what specific situation will you remind yourself that Allah alone is the Creator without need for external causes?
?
If every question about God's actions leads to theological pitfalls, what does that imply about the proper posture of a believer before His commands? Write a short prayer you can recite whenever the 'why' arises in your heart, surrendering to His absolute sovereignty.

Question Limited to Specific Meanings
Opinion that the question reduces to a few specific inquiries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a theological opinion that restricts the question 'why did God create?' to a few self-referential inquiries.

Core Idea
The question 'why did God create' can only ask about the specific world or time, which is circular.

This passage presents a third opinion in a series of arguments about whether it is valid to ask 'why did God create?'. According to this view, the question does not have many branches but reduces to a couple of limited meanings. The passage explains that asking about creation is essentially asking about the world itself or the time of its creation, making the question redundant.

Guided Path

Read the passage and translation

3
Reflect on the circular nature
Consider why the opinion says these questions are circular: creation is not other than time; asking about creation is already asking about existence itself.

Creation's Purpose Demands Divine Guidance
Maturidi argues that creation's indication of God's oneness and wisdom necessitates guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that divine guidance (shari'a) is necessary because without it, creation would be futile and Allah would lack wisdom.

Core Idea
Creation's purpose demands guidance; otherwise, it is futile.

Maturidi presents a concise rational argument linking the purpose of creation to the necessity of divine commands. He argues that since creation clearly indicates Allah's oneness and wisdom, leaving humanity without knowledge of that would render creation meaningless, which cannot be attributed to a wise Creator. This passage establishes the rational basis for the necessity of prophethood and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the two conditional statements: 'فلم يجز' and 'لما في رفع الكلفة'.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two conditional arguments. First, if there is no knowledge of God's oneness and wisdom, creation becomes futile. Second, if obligation is removed, the wisdom of creation is lost.
Each argument leads to an undesirable consequence that cannot be attributed to God.
3
Reflect on the term 'playful' (عابث)
Maturidi says that without obligation, creation would be for annihilation only, which is the action of a playful being, not a wise one. Consider why this is a strong theological point.
This connects to the attributes of wisdom and seriousness in God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن الله خلق خلقا يدل على وحدانيته وحكمته، فلم يجز إخلاء الخلق عن معرفة ذلك، فيصير خلقه عبثا. ولما في رفع الكلفة زوال حكمة الخلقة، إذ حصلت للفناء، وكل بان شيئا للنقض لا غير فهو عابت غير حكيم.
And because Allah created a creation that indicates His oneness and wisdom, it is not permissible to leave the creation without knowledge of that, for then His creation would become futile. And because in the removal of obligation there is the loss of the wisdom of creation, since it (creation) would be for annihilation, and everything that is established for nothing other than dissolution is the action of one who is playful, not wise.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is another rational proof for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions, based on the purpose of creation. Maturidi argues that Allah's creation itself points to His oneness and wisdom. If humans were left without knowledge of that (i.e., without revelation and guidance), then the creation would be futile (عبث). Furthermore, if there were no obligation (تكليف), the wisdom of creation would be lost, because creation would only lead to annihilation, and any action that exists solely for destruction is the act of a frivolous being, not a wise one. Therefore, to avoid this conclusion, commands and prohibitions must exist.

Key Terms
عبث
حكمة
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a proof for the existence of God.
Correction: The passage assumes God's existence and oneness are already known from creation; it argues for the necessity of revelation to avoid futility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen to creation if humans were left without knowledge of God's oneness and wisdom?
A) Creation would be for the best.
B) Creation would be futile.
C) Creation would be eternal.
D) Creation would be self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses rational argument to conclude that divine guidance is necessary; otherwise creation would be futile and God would not be wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that creation reflects God's oneness and wisdom, why do you still live as if this world is only for your own pleasure and annihilation? What would change if you took the obligation (الكلفة) seriously today?
?
When you ignore divine commands, are you not effectively treating creation as futile, as if God created without purpose? How will you respond to the wisdom of creation in your next decision?
?
The passage says that if obligation were removed, creation would be for annihilation only. Are you living as if obligation is optional? What is one obligation you have been avoiding, and what concrete step will you take to fulfill it?
