False Interpretations of Self-Knowledge
Maturidi enumerates four false interpretations of self-knowledge leading to knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four erroneous interpretations of the dictum 'he who knows himself knows his Lord' as critiqued by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
False views project human limitations onto God.

Imam Maturidi begins by noting that all rational people agree on the famous saying, yet they differ on its meaning. He then presents four incorrect interpretations: those of the Dualists, Jews, Anthropomorphists, and Jahm. This lesson examines each view and why Maturidi rejects it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage listing false views
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the four groups and their claims.
The views are presented sequentially.
2
Identify each group and their error
For each group, note what feature of the self they project onto God. Dualists project duality, Jews project composition, Anthropomorphists project corporeality, Jahm projects temporality.
3
Reflect on the pattern
All false interpretations make God similar to human limitations. This projection is the core error Maturidi wants to avoid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التوحيد [معرفة الرب] سببها، فإن قال قائل : أجمع العقلاء على أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة . فقالت الثنوية : لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيرته واحد جزء . وقالت المشبهة : هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم. وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن، وعرف أنه شيء، جسم، عالم، سميع، بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الاسم فهو حدث، وربه الذي أنشأه لا يُحتمل أن يكون حدثا . وعندنا أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل بما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك بإصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذ هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها. فهذا شأنه؛ مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه. فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها - مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز . علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليها؛ بل لو كان الأمر إليه لدبرها على ما يلزم أن يعلم جميع ذلك. إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيها أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها . فيعلم عند ذلك إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيرا فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكا لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفا على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور فيعلم خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين، إذ هم تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه. ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من الاحتمال والوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعاني التي عليها نفسه، وفيها تقلبها. فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره.
A question on Tawhid: Knowledge of the Lord. Its cause: For if a questioner says: The rational people agree that whoever knows himself knows his Lord; but they differed in the aspect of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knew that his self comprises good and evil, he knew that for each direction there is a Lord. The Jews made Him one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body; because in the witnessed world, self-knowledge is of a body. Jahm said: When he knew that he existed after not existing, and he knew that he is a thing, a body, knowing, hearing, seeing, he knew that everything that has that name is temporal, and his Lord who originated it cannot be temporal. According to us, whoever knows himself knows his Lord: because he knows it through ignorance of what it comprises of hearing, sight, and other accidents, and likewise through repairing what is corrupted of it, and through the amount it occupies of time and space, and through the kinds of needs that come upon it whose source and true nature of their removal it does not know. Such is its state; along with what witnesses its removal from what it witnessed of itself. His knowledge of what has passed of its states from the beginning of its existence to its current state—along with knowledge of the varying states until its cessation—is more distant, and more difficult for his imagination to conceive, and more incapable for his intellect to encompass. He knows by necessity that he did not manage his own self as it is; rather, if the matter were in his hands, he would have managed it so as to know all that. For if there were any power over anything of that, it would not have led to the ignorance that is established, then to the incapacity in it informed of repelling needs from itself and repairing what is corrupted of it. He knows at that point—since he is the most capable of creatures in managing what he perceives, and the highest in perceiving realities, and the quickest in understanding what is known and remembered of matters—he knows his exit from managing himself in origination, annihilation, and preservation, then from manifesting all the perceptibles, for they are under his management as if bewildered in their needs. And he knows that someone like him, given his capacity to understand and grasp matters and perceive causes, can only be from one who is beyond all the meanings that his self possesses and in which it fluctuates. So he knows that He is a Powerful who does not become incapable, a Knowing who does not become ignorant, and a Compeller who is not disputed in His management.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a sustained theological argument on the famous dictum 'He who knows himself knows his Lord.' Maturidi structures it as a response to an objector who notes that all rational people agree on this saying but differ in interpretation. First, he presents four erroneous views: (1) The Dualists (Thunawiyya) think that since the self contains both good and evil, there must be two lords, one for each. (2) The Jews (Yahud) reduce Him to a single part, implying composition. (3) The Anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) claim God is a body because self-knowledge in the physical world is knowledge of a body. (4) Jahm ibn Safwan holds that when one knows himself as a temporal created thing with attributes like body, knowledge, hearing, sight, he infers that whatever has those names is temporal, so God cannot be described with them. Maturidi then presents the correct understanding ('according to us'). The true self-knowledge leads to God through recognizing one's own limitations: ignorance of the inner workings of senses and accidents, need for repair, limitation in time and space, dependence on unknown causes for needs, and the witnessed fact of death and decay. He then argues that one's knowledge of the entire history of the self is impossible to fully grasp, showing that one cannot have designed or managed oneself. If one had power, one would not have ignorance and inability. Therefore, since humans are the most capable of managing what they perceive yet cannot manage themselves, they must depend on a Creator who is beyond all their attributes—powerful without weakness, knowing without ignorance, sovereign without contest. This reasoning is a 'from contingency' argument: the self's limitations point to a transcendent source.

Key Terms
ثنوية
يهود
مشبهة
جهم
عقلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dictum means God is the same as the self.
Correction: Maturidi shows that false views interpret it literally or project human attributes onto God, but the correct view distinguishes Creator from creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which group says that because the self contains good and evil, there must be two lords?
A) Dualists
B) Jews
C) Anthropomorphists
D) Jahm

Key Takeaway
False views about self-knowledge lead to false conceptions of God, such as dualism, corporealism, or denial of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own soul, do you find any hidden tendency to conceive of God as limited, like a part, a body, or as indifferent to your good and evil deeds? How will you actively uproot such subtle shirk from your heart starting today?
?
Jahm’s error was to deny God’s attributes because he saw them as temporal in himself. Are there any attributes of God that you struggle to affirm because they feel too human? What step will you take to embrace God’s perfection without falling into anthropomorphism?
?
The Dualists saw good and evil in themselves and concluded there must be two lords. Do you ever live as if you serve two masters — your desires and your Lord? How will this passage remind you to serve only One, even when your soul pulls in two directions?

Maturidi's Proof of God from the Self's Limitations
Maturidi presents his argument from the self's contingency to prove God's existence and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that recognizing the self's limitations—ignorance, need, and inability—necessarily leads to knowledge of a transcendent Creator.

Core Idea
Your own limits prove you are not the master of your being.

After refuting false views, Maturidi presents the correct understanding. He argues that genuine self-knowledge reveals the self's contingency: it lacks knowledge of its own workings, cannot repair itself fully, depends on time and space, and is subject to needs and decay. This demonstrates that the self cannot have created or managed itself, pointing to a Creator who is all-powerful, all-knowing, and sovereign.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's positive argument
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'According to us' and the translation. Follow the reasoning carefully.
This is a rational proof, so pay attention to logical steps.
2
Identify the self's limitations
Maturidi lists five signs: ignorance of senses/accidents, need for repair, spatial-temporal bounds, unknown causes of needs, and mortality.
3
Understand the logical implication
These limitations show the self cannot manage itself; if it had power, it would not have these defects. Therefore, it depends on an external cause.
The argument is a form of contingency argument.
4
Conclude the Creator's attributes
That cause must be beyond all human attributes: Powerful without incapacity, Knowing without ignorance, and Sovereign without contest.
These are the attributes Maturidi emphasizes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في التوحيد [معرفة الرب] سببها، فإن قال قائل : أجمع العقلاء على أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لكنهم اختلفوا في وجه المعرفة . فقالت الثنوية : لما عرف اشتمال نفسه على الخير والشر عرف أن لكل جهة منه رباً. واليهود صيرته واحد جزء . وقالت المشبهة : هو جسم؛ إذ في الشاهد تكون معرفة النفس للجسم. وقال جهم: إذ عرف أنه كان بعد أن لم يكن، وعرف أنه شيء، جسم، عالم، سميع، بصير، علم أن كل ما كان له ذلك الاسم فهو حدث، وربه الذي أنشأه لا يُحتمل أن يكون حدثا . وعندنا أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل بما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك بإصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذ هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها. فهذا شأنه؛ مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه. فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها - مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز . علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليها؛ بل لو كان الأمر إليه لدبرها على ما يلزم أن يعلم جميع ذلك. إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيها أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها . فيعلم عند ذلك إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيرا فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكا لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفا على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور فيعلم خروجه من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين، إذ هم تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه. ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من الاحتمال والوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعاني التي عليها نفسه، وفيها تقلبها. فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره.
A question on Tawhid: Knowledge of the Lord. Its cause: For if a questioner says: The rational people agree that whoever knows himself knows his Lord; but they differed in the aspect of knowledge. The Dualists said: When he knew that his self comprises good and evil, he knew that for each direction there is a Lord. The Jews made Him one part. The Anthropomorphists said: He is a body; because in the witnessed world, self-knowledge is of a body. Jahm said: When he knew that he existed after not existing, and he knew that he is a thing, a body, knowing, hearing, seeing, he knew that everything that has that name is temporal, and his Lord who originated it cannot be temporal. According to us, whoever knows himself knows his Lord: because he knows it through ignorance of what it comprises of hearing, sight, and other accidents, and likewise through repairing what is corrupted of it, and through the amount it occupies of time and space, and through the kinds of needs that come upon it whose source and true nature of their removal it does not know. Such is its state; along with what witnesses its removal from what it witnessed of itself. His knowledge of what has passed of its states from the beginning of its existence to its current state—along with knowledge of the varying states until its cessation—is more distant, and more difficult for his imagination to conceive, and more incapable for his intellect to encompass. He knows by necessity that he did not manage his own self as it is; rather, if the matter were in his hands, he would have managed it so as to know all that. For if there were any power over anything of that, it would not have led to the ignorance that is established, then to the incapacity in it informed of repelling needs from itself and repairing what is corrupted of it. He knows at that point—since he is the most capable of creatures in managing what he perceives, and the highest in perceiving realities, and the quickest in understanding what is known and remembered of matters—he knows his exit from managing himself in origination, annihilation, and preservation, then from manifesting all the perceptibles, for they are under his management as if bewildered in their needs. And he knows that someone like him, given his capacity to understand and grasp matters and perceive causes, can only be from one who is beyond all the meanings that his self possesses and in which it fluctuates. So he knows that He is a Powerful who does not become incapable, a Knowing who does not become ignorant, and a Compeller who is not disputed in His management.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a sustained theological argument on the famous dictum 'He who knows himself knows his Lord.' Maturidi structures it as a response to an objector who notes that all rational people agree on this saying but differ in interpretation. First, he presents four erroneous views: (1) The Dualists (Thunawiyya) think that since the self contains both good and evil, there must be two lords, one for each. (2) The Jews (Yahud) reduce Him to a single part, implying composition. (3) The Anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) claim God is a body because self-knowledge in the physical world is knowledge of a body. (4) Jahm ibn Safwan holds that when one knows himself as a temporal created thing with attributes like body, knowledge, hearing, sight, he infers that whatever has those names is temporal, so God cannot be described with them. Maturidi then presents the correct understanding ('according to us'). The true self-knowledge leads to God through recognizing one's own limitations: ignorance of the inner workings of senses and accidents, need for repair, limitation in time and space, dependence on unknown causes for needs, and the witnessed fact of death and decay. He then argues that one's knowledge of the entire history of the self is impossible to fully grasp, showing that one cannot have designed or managed oneself. If one had power, one would not have ignorance and inability. Therefore, since humans are the most capable of managing what they perceive yet cannot manage themselves, they must depend on a Creator who is beyond all their attributes—powerful without weakness, knowing without ignorance, sovereign without contest. This reasoning is a 'from contingency' argument: the self's limitations point to a transcendent source.

Key Terms
علم ضرورة
قادر
عالم
جبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge means the self is eternal or divine.
Correction: Maturidi shows self-knowledge reveals dependency, not divinity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the self's inability to repair itself prove?
A) The self is eternal.
B) The self has power.
C) The self is dependent on a Creator.
D) The self is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the self's clear limitations to prove the existence of a Creator who is absolutely powerful, knowing, and sovereign.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation beyond your control—illness, uncertainty, or weakness—do you truly acknowledge that you are not the master of your own being, or do you still cling to the illusion of self-sufficiency? What would it look like to live in constant awareness of your dependence on the Creator?
?
Maturidi argues that if you had power over yourself, you would have removed ignorance and need. Look at a specific area of your life where you repeatedly fail or struggle. Why do you still try to manage it on your own instead of turning to the One who is truly Powerful and Knowing?
?
The proof concludes that the Creator is a Compeller not disputed in His management. Is there an area in your life where you resist His management—where you argue with His decree or wish things were different? Will you surrender that area today and acknowledge His absolute sovereignty?

Knowing Oneself Leads to Knowing God
Maturidi argues that self-knowledge of limitations proves God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses self-reflection on human limitations to prove the existence and attributes of God.

Core Idea
Seeing what we cannot do points us to the One who can.

Imam Maturidi presents a profound argument that begins with introspection. By honestly examining our own limitations—our ignorance of how our bodies work, our inability to fix what goes wrong, our lack of control over time and space—we realize that we are not self-sufficient. This realization leads inevitably to the recognition of a Creator who is all-powerful, all-knowing, and sovereign. In this passage, Maturidi lays out this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Focus on the overall flow of the argument without analyzing every detail yet.
The passage is dense but coherent; let the author's rhythm guide you.
2
Identify the human limitations listed
Maturidi mentions several specific ways in which humans are limited: ignorance of sensory faculties, inability to correct bodily corruption, lack of control over time and space, and inability to know the source of needs. Notice each one.
These limitations are presented as evidence of our dependence.
3
Trace the conclusion
Observe how from these limitations Maturidi concludes that humans are not the true managers of themselves. He then infers that there must be a being outside of these limitations—God—who is powerful, knowing, and sovereign.
This is a rational proof, not merely introspection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا أن من عرف نفسه عرف ربه؛ لما يعرفها بالجهل بما احتملته هي من السمع والبصر وغيرهما من الأعراض، وكذلك بإصلاح ما فسد منها، وبقدر ما تأخذ هي من الزمان والمكان، وبأنواع حاجات ترد عليها لا يعرف مأتاها ولا حقيقة ما به زوالها. فهذا شأنه؛ مع ما يشهد زوالها بما شهد من نفسه. فعلمه بما مضى من أحوالها من أول ما كانت إلى الحال التي هو فيها - مع العلم فيما يختلف عليها من الأحوال إلى وقت قيامها منه ـ أبعد، وعن تصور ذلك في وهمه أعسر، وعن احتمال إحاطة عقله به أعجز. علم بضرورة أنه لم يدبر أمر نفسه على ما هي عليه؛ بل لو كان الأمر إليه لدبرها على ما يلزم أن يعلم جميع ذلك. إذ لو كانت ثمة قدرة على شيء من ذلك لم يكن ليدفع إلى الجهل الذي ثبت، ثم إلى العجز فيها أخبرت من دفع الحاجات عن نفسه، وإصلاح ما فسد منها. فيعلم عند ذلك ـ إذ هو أملك الخلائق تدبيرا فيما يحس، وأعلاهم إدراكا لحقائق ما يلقى، وأسرعهم وقوفا على ما يعلم ويذكر من الأمور - فيعلم خروجه: من تدبير نفسه في التكوين والإفناء والإبقاء، ثم من إبداء جميع المحسوسين، إذ هم تحت تدبيره كالمتحيرين في حوائجه. ويعلم بأن مثله على ما عليه من الاحتمال والوقوف على الأمور والإدراك للأسباب لا يكون إلا بمن هو خارج من جميع المعاني التي عليها نفسه، وفيها تقلبها. فيعلم أنه بقادر لا يعجز، وعالم لا يجهل، وجبار لا ينازع في تدبيره.
And with us, whoever knows himself knows his Lord; for he knows it through ignorance of what it has taken on from hearing, sight, and other accidents, and likewise through the correction of what corrupts from it, and through the extent of what it takes from time and place, and through the types of needs that come upon it for which it does not know the source nor the reality of what removes them. This is its state; along with what it witnesses of its passing away by what it witnesses of itself. So his knowledge of what has passed of its states from its beginning to the state he is in—along with knowledge of what varies upon it of states until the time of its standing from it—is farther [away], and for the conception of that in his imagination it is more difficult, and for the possibility of encompassing it with his intellect it is more incapable. He knows by necessity that it has not managed its own affair as it is; but if the matter were up to it, it would manage it as it must know all that. For if there were any power over any of that, it would not be driven to the ignorance that is established, then to incapacity in it from repelling needs from itself and correcting what corrupts from it. So he knows at that point—since he is the most capable of creatures in managing what he perceives, and the highest of them in grasping the realities of what he encounters, and the quickest of them in comprehending what is known and mentioned of affairs—he knows his departure from managing himself in formation, annihilation, and preservation, then from manifesting all the perceived things, for they are under his management like the bewildered in their needs. And he knows that the like of him, upon what he is of capacity and comprehension of affairs and perception of causes, is not except from one who is outside all the meanings upon which his self is, and in which it changes. So he knows that He is a Powerful One who does not become incapable, a Knowing One who does not become ignorant, and a Compeller who is not disputed in His management.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence and attributes of God based on self-reflection. Imam Maturidi begins by stating that knowing oneself leads to knowing one's Lord. He then explains that a person knows their self through several types of ignorance and incapacity: ignorance of how sensory faculties work, inability to fix what goes wrong, lack of control over time and space, and inability to know the source or removal of needs. The person also witnesses the transience of their own states. Their knowledge of past and future states is extremely limited and difficult to conceive. Thus, by necessity, they realize that they are not the true manager of themselves; if they were, they would have full knowledge and control. The fact that they cannot prevent ignorance and weakness shows they lack power. Furthermore, even the most capable human is limited in managing even basic biological processes and needs. This leads to the conclusion that such a dependent and limited being must be created and sustained by something outside itself—a being that is powerful, knowing, and sovereign. Thus, the proof ends with affirming that God is powerful (qadir), knowing (alim), and compeller (jabbar), without change or dispute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies solely on introspection and subjective experience.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is based on observable, universal human limitations—such as ignorance of sensory processes and inability to control bodily functions—which are rational and objective grounds for inference.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what does the human inability to fully manage their own body and needs prove?
A) That humans are inherently flawed
B) That a powerful, knowing Creator must exist
C) That humans have no free will
D) That the self is eternal

Key Takeaway
Human self-awareness of our own limits leads necessarily to the recognition of a limitless Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life—your ignorance of how your body heals, your inability to control time, your dependence on factors you don't fully understand—do you actually live as if you are in control? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not the manager of your own existence?
?
This lesson says that even the most capable human is like 'the bewildered in their needs' when it comes to managing basic functions. How does that confront your pride in your achievements? In what areas of your life are you still trying to be self-sufficient instead of acknowledging your need for a Powerful, Knowing, and Sovereign Lord?
?
Maturidi argues that recognizing your limits leads directly to recognizing God's perfect attributes. But do you resist that conclusion? Are there parts of your life where you prefer to believe you are self-managing, perhaps because it feels safer or more comfortable to rely on yourself? What would it cost you to fully surrender your self-management to the One who never becomes incapable, ignorant, or challenged?

Acceptance of Varying States Points to a Wise Maker
Maturidi argues from the capacity for change to a wise maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the capacity for change and acquisition to argue for a wise Creator and against blind natural causes.

Core Idea
Capacity for change proves a wise maker, not blind nature.

Maturidi presents a signature argument from design. He points to the fact that things can accept different states (like knowledge, power) by choice, and also can be good or evil. This, he argues, cannot be the work of mindless natures but must be from a knowing Maker who is beyond such capacity and diversity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the two key phrases: 'بما ذكرتُ من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختياراً' and 'باحتماله الخير والشر'.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage presents two lines of evidence: 1) things accept states by choice, and 2) things accept good and evil. Identify each and notice how they work together.
3
See how the conclusion follows
From these premises, Maturidi concludes that the maker must be knowing and that management is from God alone. Ask yourself why natures cannot explain this.
The phrase 'لا بالطبائع' is key – Maturidi is explicitly rejecting naturalistic explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بما ذكرتُ من إمكان قبول الأحوال اختياراً، و احتماله الصفات العلية من نحو العلم والقدرة والحياة والسمع والبصر ما يوضح كونه بالصانع العليم، لا بالطبائع التي هي عاجزة عن الاختيار وجاهلة بالأحوال؛ وكذلك جميع الأغذية ولا قوة إلا بالله. وكذلك باحتماله الخير والشر ومختلف الأحوال دليل صرف تدبيره إلى من لا يوصف بالاحتمال ولا بمختلف الأحوال، ليكون كل شيء على ما عليه تقديره له. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And by what I mentioned of the possibility of accepting states by choice, and its capacity for lofty attributes like knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight, it clarifies that it is by the knowing Maker, not by natures that are incapable of choice and ignorant of states; and likewise all foods. And there is no power except through Allah. And similarly, by its capacity for good and evil and diverse states, it is a proof that its management is directed to Him who is not described by capacity or by diverse states, so that everything is as His decree requires. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an argument from design based on the capacity for change and acquisition. Maturidi argues that the fact that things can accept various states (like knowledge, power, etc.) by choice shows they are made by a knowing Maker (الصانع العليم), not by blind natures (طبائع) that lack choice and knowledge. He also notes that the capacity for good, evil, and diverse states proves that the management of all things is directed to God alone, who is not subject to such capacity or diversity. Everything occurs according to His decree.

Correction: Maturidi argues that the capacity for change in creation actually points to a wise Creator who is beyond change and imperfection.

Key Takeaway
The capacity of created things to accept different states and to be good or evil demonstrates that they are made by a wise God, not by blind nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says that all foods and diverse states are managed by Allah. Have you ever attributed a success or failure solely to your own planning, forgetting that the capacity for any outcome is from Him? What specific change will you make this week to acknowledge His control more consciously?
?
If you believed with certainty that blind nature cannot produce the complexities of your own character and choices, how would that transform your trust in Allah's wisdom when you face difficult circumstances? What one situation will you surrender to Him today as a practical step?

Two Wisdoms in Creating Harmful Substances
Maturidi enumerates two wisdoms for creating harmful substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two wisdoms behind the creation of harmful things like snakes, as explained by Imam al-Maturidi.

Core Idea
Harmful creations serve as trials and training for the afterlife.

Imam al-Maturidi addresses a common question: why did God create harmful things like snakes? While the full wisdom of divine actions is beyond human intellect, he offers two insights. This passage shows that harmful things serve as a trial to make the consequences of obedience and disobedience tangible, and as a means to train us in dealing with enemies and friends.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording set the context before interpretation.
Notice that the author starts by acknowledging the limits of human intellect regarding divine wisdom.
2
Identify the first wisdom
Find the first wisdom mentioned: trial (miḥna) through present harm and benefit. Understand how this helps make the pleasures of reward and pains of punishment known.
The author says God made an example from the visible world so that the promised reward/punishment can be imagined.
3
Identify the second wisdom
Find the second wisdom: training in dealing with enemies and friends. Note how harmful things serve as likenesses of enemies and beneficial things as friends.
This trains believers in caution, preparation, and seeking help.
4
Reflect on the analogy to children's worship
Consider the final sentence about children being commanded to worship when able. How does this connect to the training idea?
The gradual training in worship parallels the training from dealing with harms and benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : الحكمة في خلق الحيّات والجواهر الضارة - وإن كانت العقول تقصر عن بلوغ كنه حكمة الربوبية، على ما سبق القول في لزوم الحكمة لكل شيء من الوجه الذي خلقه الله ، وإن لم يعرف مائيتها - يكون من وجوه. الأول، المحنة بالضار والنافع الحاضرين، ليُعْلَم بهما لذة الثواب على الطاعة وألم العقاب على المعصية. إذ الخلق جبلوا على قصد العواقب في الأفعال. فجعل لها مثالاً من العيان ليتصور الموعود في الأوهام، فيسهل به السبيل. والله الموفق. والثاني، أن المحنة هي تحمّل المؤنة التي تسهل وتصعب على البدن بالنظر والفكر. والناس في تكلّف النظر والفكر يختلفون؛ لأنه ليست لهما منفعة حاضرة، وبهما الشغل عن اللذات والشهوات. وتحمّل مثله على البدن عسير، وفي التقصير فيهما اختلاف وتفرق، وذلك يُعقِب المعاداة والمجاوبة، وفي الموافقة موالاة ومسالمة. فجعل الله تعالى لهم فيما خلق لهم شبيه الأعداء بما فيها من المضار، ومثال الأولياء بما فيها من المنافع، ليكون بشر زاجرا لهم على اعتياد كيفية معاملات الأعداء والأولياء؛ حتى إذا ابْتُلُوا بمثله في جوهرهم عرفوا كيفيته من الحذر والتأهب والمعونة والنصر. وعلى ذلك يؤمر الصبيان عند احتمال وسعهم العبادات
The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The wisdom in creating snakes and harmful substances—even if intellects fall short of attaining the reality of the wisdom of lordship, as previously stated about the necessity of wisdom for everything from the aspect in which God created it, even if its 'whatness' is not known—is from [several] aspects. First, the trial (miḥna) through present harm and benefit, so that by them the pleasure of reward for obedience and the pain of punishment for disobedience are known. For creatures are disposed to desire consequences in actions. So He made for them an example from the visible world so that the promised [reward or punishment] might be pictured in imaginations, thereby facilitating the path. And God is the Granter of success. Second, that the trial is the undertaking of burden that becomes easy or difficult for the body through reflection and thought. People differ in undertaking reflection and thought because they have no immediate benefit, and they occupy one away from pleasures and desires. Undertaking such difficulty for the body is hard, and in falling short in them there is disagreement and division, and that leads to enmity and quarrel, while in agreement there is friendship and peace. So God made for them, in what He created for them, a likeness of enemies in the harms therein, and an example of friends in the benefits therein, so that it might be a good deterrent for them to accustom themselves to the manner of dealing with enemies and friends; so that when they are tested with the like in their own substance, they know its manner in terms of caution, preparation, assistance, and victory. And accordingly, children are commanded, when they bear the capacity, [to perform] acts of worship.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage offers two wisdoms for the creation of harmful things like snakes. First, they serve as a trial (miḥna) through mixing harm and benefit in this world, so that people can experience a taste of the pleasure of reward and the pain of punishment, making the promised afterlife more vivid and motivating obedience. Second, the difficulty of reflection and thought (which are hard because they have no immediate benefit) is used to train people in dealing with enemies and friends. God created harmful things as a likeness of enemies and beneficial things as a likeness of friends, so that people learn caution, preparation, and how to seek help. This training is analogous to how children are gradually commanded to perform acts of worship when they are able.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Harmful creations are pointless or purely evil.
Correction: This passage shows that harmful things serve important wisdoms: as trials to make consequences vivid and as training in dealing with enemies and friends.

Checkpoint
What is the second wisdom mentioned for creating harmful things?
A) To cause fear among people
B) To train people in dealing with enemies and friends
C) To punish sinners directly
D) To test the intellect of scholars

Key Takeaway
God created harmful things for wise purposes: as trials to make the afterlife vivid and as training to deal with enemies and friends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful or harmful situation, do you immediately see it as pointless suffering, or could you recognize it as a training ground for dealing with enemies and seeking God's help? What one habit can you change this week to prepare for such trials?
?
The passage says children are commanded to worship when they have capacity. What burden are you avoiding because it has no immediate benefit? How does reflecting on the second wisdom—training for the afterlife—motivate you to embrace that burden starting today?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Diverse Creation Points to One Wise Creator
Maturidi presents the first proof from diverse substances indicating one Manager.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the diversity of created substances, despite their differences in harm and benefit, as evidence for a single wise Creator and His oneness.

Core Idea
Opposites in creation unite to testify to one Creator.

Maturidi presents a profound argument: the vast variety of substances in creation—some harmful, some beneficial—all equally point to a single wise Manager. This is not coincidental; it is a deliberate sign of divine wisdom and unity. In this passage, you will see how even contradictions in nature serve the same testimony of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on diversity
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأيضا إن الخلق' to 'بربوبيته واحداً' along with its translation. Notice how Maturidi emphasizes that despite differences, all substances agree in indicating one Creator.
The phrase 'كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق' (like a single substance in agreement) is key.
2
Understand the argument
Reflect on why the diversity of substances—harmful and beneficial alike—strengthens the proof for God's oneness rather than weakening it. Maturidi calls it 'بيان عجيب حكمته' (a wondrous explanation of His wisdom).
3
Connect to tawhid
Consider how this argument refutes polytheism or dualism: if there were multiple gods, the chaotic diversity would not point to a single, wise plan. Ask yourself: How does the coexistence of opposites testify to a single Planner?
This is a hallmark of Maturidi's rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأخلاق المحمودة للاعتياد ليسهل سبيل ذلك عليهم وقت التكليف، فمثله في خلق ما ذكر. والله أعلم. وأيضا إن الخلق ـ على اختلاف جوهرهم في المضار والمنافع ـ جعلهم الله في الدلالة على مدبّر لهم حكيم عليم وعلى وحدانيته كجوهر واحد في الاتفاق من جهة الدلالة والشهادة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. فيكون في ذلك بيان عجيب حكمته : أن جمع جمع بين الضار والنافع والخير والشر، على تناقضهما في الدلالة على وحدانيته والشهادة بربوبيته واحداً. وأيضا إنه خلق ذلك ليذلّل به الجبابرة والملوك، فيعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، ولئلا يغتروا بكثرة الحواشي والجنود فيتعدوا حدود الله ؛ بما يرون من سلطان في قدرته: تسليط من يشاء على من يشاء. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وليُعْلَم مِن تأمل خلقه على جوهر الضرر والنفع على غناه وتعاليه عن أن تمسته الحاجات؛ لأن من ذلك وصفه فإنما يخرج فعله على وجوه تنفع ولا تضر . وليعلم قدرته على ما يشاء. مع ما لا يشاهد من الجواهر الضارة إلا وفيه منافع تعجز الخلائق عن الإحاطة بكنهها. ما فيها من الإحراق، ففيها من إصلاح الأغذية. والماء يجوز أن يكون من ذلك النار، مع به حياة كل ذي روح وهلاكه. وكذلك [لا يوجد] جوهر مُرّ أو سام إلا [و] فيه دواء [للداء / المعضل]؛ ليعلم الناظر أن القول بالشر بالجوهر أو الخير خطأ باطل، بل كل جوهر منه ضر ونفع، فيكون في ذلك أعظم آيات التوحيد. ما فيه وجهان، أحدهما القدرة التامة على ملك ما يضر وينفع ليُرْجَى ويُخاف. مع ومن لا يكون كذلك لا يتم الأمر به؛ لأنه لا يُرْهَب منه ولا يُرْغَب فيها عنده، وقد يغلبه من له الأمران أيضاً. والثاني ليتم العبر وليصح الأمر والنهي، فيكون للنظر والفكر مجال في الأمرين؛ ولأنهما عظة بهما وعبرة ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the praiseworthy morals through habituation, so that the path of that becomes easy for them at the time of obligation, so the like of that is in the creation of what was mentioned. And God knows best. And also, indeed creation—despite the difference of their substances in harms and benefits—God made them in indication of a wise, knowing Manager for them and of His oneness, like a single substance in agreement from the aspect of indication and testimony. And there is no power except with God. So in that is a wondrous explanation of His wisdom: that He gathered together the harmful and the beneficial, the good and the evil, despite their contradiction, in indicating His oneness and testifying to His lordship as one. And also, He created that to humble the tyrants and kings, so that they may know thereby their weakness, and so that they not be deluded by the abundance of retinues and armies and thus transgress the bounds of God, by what they see of authority in His power: giving power over whom He wills to whom He wills. And there is no power except with God. And so that it may be known, from contemplating His creation upon the substance of harm and benefit, His self-sufficiency and His transcendence above being touched by needs; because from that is His description, so His action only comes forth in ways that benefit and do not harm. And so that His power over what He wills may be known. Along with what is not witnessed of harmful substances except that in them are benefits that creatures are incapable of encompassing their reality. What is in them of burning, in them is the improvement of foods. And water may be from that fire, with it is the life of every living soul and its destruction. And likewise, there is no bitter or poisonous substance except that in it is a cure for the chronic disease; so that the observer may know that the statement that evil belongs to the substance or good is a false error, rather every substance from it is harm and benefit, so in that are the greatest signs of tawhid. It contains two aspects: one is the complete power over possessing what harms and benefits, so that He may be hoped and feared. And one who is not like that, the matter is not completed by him; because he is not feared from nor desired with him, and the one who has both matters may overcome him. And the second is so that lessons are completed and command and prohibition are valid, so that there is room for reflection and thought in the two matters; and because they are an admonition and a lesson, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument from the diversity of created substances in benefit and harm to the existence of a single wise Creator. The author begins by noting that God made all created substances—despite their differences in harm and benefit—equally indicate a single wise Manager and His oneness. This is a wondrous wisdom: God combined opposites (harmful and beneficial, good and evil) to testify to His oneness. Then he adds that God created this to humble tyrants and kings, so they realize their weakness and do not transgress God's bounds—since God gives power to whom He wills. Further, by contemplating creation, one learns of God's self-sufficiency and transcendence above needs; His actions only benefit, not harm. Also, one learns His absolute power. The author then points out that every harmful substance contains benefits beyond human comprehension (e.g., fire burns but also cooks food; water gives life but can also cause death). Likewise, every bitter or poisonous substance contains a cure. Therefore, the notion that a substance is purely evil or purely good is false; every substance has both harm and benefit. This is a great sign of tawhid. Finally, he lists two wisdoms behind this: (1) to show God's complete power over harm and benefit so that He alone is hoped and feared; (2) to enable bidding and forbidding, giving room for reflection and admonition.

نفع
وحدانية
مدبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that the existence of harmful or evil substances indicates multiple creators or a dualistic principle (e.g., a good god and an evil god).
Correction: Maturidi argues that the very coexistence of harmful and beneficial substances, all pointing to one Creator, proves the oneness of God and refutes dualism.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the relationship between diverse substances (harmful and beneficial) and the oneness of God?
A) They point to different creators for each type.
B) They are all equal in indicating one wise Manager and His oneness.
C) Only beneficial substances indicate God's oneness.
D) Harmful substances indicate an evil creator.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of creation, including opposites, is a unified testimony to the oneness and wisdom of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a loss or harm, do you still see that event as a testimony to Allah's oneness, or does your heart tend to attribute it to a separate source? What would it take to truly believe that all things—good and bad—come from the same wise Manager?
?
The passage says that God 'gathered together the harmful and the beneficial' to testify to His oneness. Have you ever deliberately reflected on the contradictions in your own life (joy and sorrow, gain and loss) as a unified sign of His wisdom? If not, what is one step you can take today to practice this perspective?
?
If every substance in creation unanimously points to one Creator, why do you sometimes fail to see that unity? Is it because you are looking at the differences instead of the deeper agreement? How can you train your eyes to see the oneness behind the diversity?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
7 min
Every Substance Contains Both Harm and Benefit
Maturidi shows that every substance contains both harm and benefit, refuting dualism, and gives two wisdoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that no created substance is purely evil or purely good; rather, every substance contains both harm and benefit, which is a greater sign of tawhid and refutes dualism.

Core Idea
No substance is purely harmful or purely beneficial—both coexist.

Maturidi deepens his argument by examining the nature of substances themselves. He points out that even in harmful things like fire or poison, there are hidden benefits. This leads to a powerful conclusion: the idea that evil or good belongs to a substance as such is false. Instead, every substance carries both aspects, making the case for tawhid even stronger and refuting dualistic worldviews.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on harm and benefit
Read from 'مع ما لا يشاهد' to 'أعظم آيات التوحيد' in Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi gives examples: fire burns but also cooks food; water gives life but can destroy; poison has a cure.
The examples are meant to show that harmful substances also contain benefits beyond human comprehension.
2
Understand the refutation of dualism
Maturidi explicitly says that the claim that evil belongs to the substance or good is a false error. Why? Because every substance has both harm and benefit. This refutes any belief in a dualistic principle where some substances are purely evil.
This is a direct attack on Manichaeism or similar dualistic theologies.
3
Reflect on the two wisdoms
The passage concludes with two wisdoms behind this creation: (1) to show God's complete power over harm and benefit so He alone is hoped and feared, and (2) to enable command and prohibition and provide lessons. Consider how these wisdoms strengthen the overall argument for tawhid.
These two aspects are explained as 'وجهان' (two aspects).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that certain substances (e.g., fire, poison) are purely evil, and others (e.g., water, food) are purely good, leading to a dualistic view where evil originates from a separate substance or principle.
Correction: Maturidi shows that every substance contains both harm and benefit, so evil is not intrinsic to any substance; thus dualism is refuted.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the statement 'evil belongs to the substance' or 'good belongs to the substance' a false error?
A) Because substances have no inherent qualities.
B) Because every substance contains both harm and benefit.
C) Because only God is good.
D) Because substances are only means, not sources.

Key Takeaway
Each created substance contains both harm and benefit, refuting dualism and affirming God's sole power over all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you only see the harm in your trials, or do you actively seek the hidden benefit that God has placed there? What trial are you facing right now that you need to reconsider as a mixture of harm and benefit for your faith?
?
Since God alone controls both harm and benefit, who or what do you fear or hope in besides Him? Reflect on a situation where you placed your hope in something other than God—how does this lesson call you to redirect your trust?
?
The coexistence of opposites in creation is a sign for reflection. When was the last time you paused to reflect on the wisdom behind a painful experience? Commit to a specific time this week to meditate on one such experience using this lens.

The Testimony of Created Substances
Maturidi introduces human disagreement and argues from the testimony of substances for a single Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the very nature of created things points to a single, wise Manager, and why human disagreement stems from ignoring this clear testimony.

Core Idea
Created things themselves testify to a single Manager.

Maturidi begins this section by pondering why humans disagree despite overwhelming evidence. He argues that every substance in the world, by its very nature, declares its dependence on a wise Creator. This lesson explores that argument and its implications for understanding divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening inquiry
Start by reading the passage from 'أما بعد' to the end. Notice how Maturidi frames the problem of human disagreement after clear signs.
The preamble is devotional; the main argument begins after 'أما بعد'.
2
Identify the testimony of substances
Find the sentence starting with 'إذ ما من شيء من جواهر العالم'. List what every substance testifies to: that it is managed, created, and in need of a Knower, Self-Sufficient, Wise Creator.
3
Understand the impossibility of multiple managers
Examine why a substance cannot be referred to multiple managers. Maturidi says it would lead to contradiction and corruption unless one dominates. Focus on the phrase 'لئلا يتناقض فيتبدد'.
Think about the logical outcome of multiple independent wills.
4
Connect to the need for a single Creator
The argument concludes that the only coherent explanation is a single, wise Manager. Note how Maturidi emphasizes that the need for a Creator at the beginning is even greater than for continuance.
This reinforces tawhid as the foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اختلاف البشر في العالم نبتدأ بالحمد الله العلي الحميد، ونتوجه إليه بالشكر له والتمجيد على ما أيدنا به من التسديد، ونرغب إليه في العون على ما قَصَدْنا له والتأييد، فإنه على كل شيء شهيد؛ ونسأله أن يصلي على محمد أفضل ما صلى على أحد من خيار خلقه، وأن يعطيه سُؤلَه ، وأن يلحقنا به بجوده، فإنه غني كريم. { قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : } أما بعد، فإني تأملت وجه اختلاف البشر في العالم بعد ظهور آيات حدثه، وأدلة جرى تدبير غيره عليه. إذ ما من شيء من جواهر العالم وأركانه إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بأنه مُدبَّر مفطور ؛ وأنه مضطر إلى عليم بأحواله، غني يملك حوائجه، حكيم يضع كل شيء موضعه؛ لئلا يتناقض فيتبدّد؛ وأنه لا يحتمل بجوهره أن يرجع إلى عدد من المدبّرين، بما لديه تمكن الاختلاف الذي عنده يريد كل أن يُظهر سلطانه، ويغلب مُلْكه، ويقهر كل من نازعه، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد. اللهم إلا أن يكون لواحد منهم فضل قوة أو نصر يخضع له الجميع، فيصير كل خاضعا له ذليلاً: بمعنى كل جوهر من جواهر العالم تنشأ [يكون] في خروجه على مشيئة غيره وجزيه عليه سلطانه . وهو المعني بالذي هو دليل مدبّر للعالم ، عليم حكيم، ليقوم به هو ويتم ويخرج من العدم إلى الوجود، إذ الأعجوبة [التي الحاجة] في ابتداء كونه إلى من يعلم كيفية إنشاء الأشياء ليست بدون الأعجوبة عن في دوامه وقيامه على ما هو عليه . بل كانت أظهر، والحاجة في ذلك إلى غيره أعظم ; إذ هو عن تدبير نفسه أعجز، وأسباب إحالته عليه أظهر . مع ما في كل براهين كونه بعد أن لم
Question: The difference of humans in the world. We begin with praise of God, the High, the Praiseworthy, and we turn to Him with thanks and glorification for what He has supported us with of right guidance, and we desire from Him help and support for what we have intended, for He is over all things a Witness; and we ask Him to bless Muhammad with the best prayer He has ever prayed upon any of the best of His creation, and to grant him his request, and to join us with him through His generosity, for He is Self-Sufficient, Generous. {The jurist Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said:} Now then, I have pondered the aspect of the difference of humans in the world after the appearance of the signs of its temporal origination, and the proofs of the running of the governance of other than it upon it. For there is no thing from among the substances of the world and its pillars except that it testifies by its substance that it is managed and created; and that it is in need of a Knower of its states, Self-Sufficient who owns its needs, Wise who places everything in its proper place; lest it contradict and disintegrate; and that it cannot by its substance be referred to a number of managers, because within it is the possibility of difference, by which each one wants to manifest his authority, and overcome his kingdom, and overpower all who contend with him, and in that is annihilation and corruption. Unless one of them has an excess of power or victory to which all submit, so that each becomes submissive and humbled to him: meaning every substance from among the substances of the world arises [being] in its coming forth upon the will of other than it and its recompense upon his authority. And that is what is meant by the one who is a proof of a Manager of the world, Knowing, Wise, so that it may subsist by Him and be completed and come out from non-existence into existence. For the wonder [the need] in the beginning of its existence for one who knows the manner of creating things is not less than the wonder concerning its continuance and subsisting upon what it is. Rather, it was more apparent, and the need in that for other than it is greater; for it is more incapable of managing itself, and the causes of its referral to Him are more apparent. Along with what is in every proof of its existence after it did not [exist]...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a new section in which Maturidi investigates the reason for human disagreement despite clear evidence of creation. After an introductory praise of God and blessing on the Prophet, he states his inquiry: he has pondered the aspect of human difference after the appearance of signs of the world's temporal origination and proofs of its governance by another. He then argues that every substance in the world testifies by its very nature that it is managed and created (مُدبَّر مفطور) and is in need of a Knower, Self-Sufficient, Wise Creator. A substance cannot be referred to multiple managers because that would lead to contradiction and corruption, unless one manager dominates all others. Thus, the only coherent explanation is a single Manager who is Knowing and Wise. The need for a Creator at the beginning of existence is even greater than for its continuation, since the thing is more incapable of managing itself. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clearly aimed at establishing tawhid as the basis for resolving human disagreement.

Key Terms
اختلاف
جوهر
مدبر
مفطور
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human disagreement is due to lack of evidence or unclear signs.
Correction: Maturidi argues that evidence is clear in the testimony of substances; disagreement arises from ignoring or misinterpreting this testimony.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does every substance in the world testify to by its very nature?
A) That it is eternal and self-sufficient.
B) That it is managed and created, and in need of a Wise, Knowing Creator.
C) That it is the product of multiple independent creators.
D) That it has no purpose or governance.

Key Takeaway
Every created thing, by its very nature, points to a single, wise Creator, and human disagreement stems from neglecting this clear testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every substance around you—from the air you breathe to the phone you hold—testifies that it is managed and created. Do you live as if you truly believe that, or do you cling to the illusion of self-sufficiency? What would change today if you saw every object as a sign pointing to the One Manager?
?
Maturidi says that multiple managers would lead to 'annihilation and corruption.' What are the multiple 'managers' in your life—your ego, ambitions, relationships—that you allow to govern your heart? How can you submit to the single Manager whom creation itself declares?
?
The need for the Manager at the beginning of existence is even greater than for its continuation. When you reflect on your own beginning—your birth, your existence today—do you feel that urgency of need for the One who brought you from nothing? How does that awareness change the way you face your next decision?

Every Substance Testifies to Its Creator
Maturidi argues that every substance testifies to a single wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how every substance in the world inherently testifies to the existence of a single wise Creator who manages it.

Core Idea
Every substance proves it is created and managed, demanding a single wise Manager.

Imam Maturidi begins his argument by observing that despite clear signs of the world’s creation, people disagree. He then makes a bold claim: every substance and pillar of the world itself bears witness to being created and managed. This testimony includes the necessity of a manager who knows, is independent, and wise, and it also implies that there cannot be multiple managers, as that would lead to contradiction and ruin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very structured argument.
2
Identify the testimony claim
Maturidi says every substance (jawhar) and pillar of the world testifies by its substance that it is managed and created (madbar mafṭur). Identify this core claim.
This is the foundation of his proof.
3
Understand the attributes needed
The testimony implies the world needs a Knower (alim), a Rich independent being (ghani), and a Wise one (hakim). Reflect on why each attribute is necessary.
These attributes are derived from the world's dependency.
4
See the rejection of polytheism
Maturidi argues that if there were multiple managers, their conflicting wills would lead to annihilation and corruption. Only if one dominates does order prevail. This proves the manager must be one.
This logical argument refutes polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اختلاف البشر في العالم
نبتدأ بالحمد لله العلي الحميد، ونتوجه إليه بالشكر له والتمجيد على ما أيدنا به من التسديد، ونرغب إليه في العون على ما قصدنا له والتأييد، فإنه على كل شيء شهيد؛ ونسأله أن يصلي على محمد أفضل ما صلى على أحد من خيار خلقه، وأن يعطيه سؤله، وأن يلحقنا به بجوده، فإنه غني كريم.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : أما بعد، فإني تأملت وجه اختلاف البشر في العالم بعد ظهور آيات حدثه، وأدلة جرى تدبير غيره عليه. إذ ما من شيء من جواهر العالم وأركانه إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بأنه مدبر مفطور؛ وأنه مضطر إلى عليم بأحواله، غني يملك حوائجه، حكيم يضع كل شيء موضعه؛ لئلا يتناقض فيتبدد؛ وأنه لا يحتمل بجوهره أن يرجع إلى عدد من المدبرين، بما لديه تمكن الاختلاف الذي عنده يريد كل أن يظهر سلطانه، ويغلب ملكه، ويقهر كل من نازعه، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد. اللهم إلا أن يكون لواحد منهم فضل قوة أو نصر يخضع له الجميع، فيصير كل خاضعا له ذليلاً: بمعنى كل جوهر من جواهر العالم يكون في خروجه على مشيئة غيره وجزيه عليه سلطانه. وهو المعني بالذي هو دليل مدبر للعالم، عليم حكيم، ليقوم به هو ويتم ويخرج من العدم إلى الوجود، إذ الأعجوبة التي الحاجة في ابتداء كونه إلى من يعلم كيفية إنشاء الأشياء ليست بدون الأعجوبة في دوامه وقيامه على ما هو عليه . بل كانت أظهر، والحاجة في ذلك إلى غيره أعظم؛ إذ هو عن تدبير نفسه أعجز، وأسباب إحالته عليه أظهر . مع ما في كل براهين كونه بعد أن لم يكن أبين، إذ كل ذي عقل وبصر لعلّه يذكر ابتداءه أو تقلبه من أحوال تقدمت من الصغر واللطافة، مما إذا لم يجعل لتلك الجملة ابتداء يبطل كونه . ثم احتمال كل الوهن والضعف إلى أن يتلاشى ويبطل مما يضطره إلى العلم بكونه بعد أن لم يكن . وإذا كان ذا أمر من يملك التدبير ويعلم بالأحوال فالموات ـ الذي هو تحت تدبير الأحياء ينتفعون به من حيث لا يشعر بذلك ـ أحق بذلك . ثم دل كون الأموات على ما للأحياء بها الاستمتاع، على أن الذي دبرها هو الذي دبر الأحياء، إذ جعلها مستمتعا لهم، بها صلاحهم.
Question: The disagreement of humans concerning the world. We begin with praise to Allah, the High, the Praised, and we turn to Him with thanks and glorification for what He has supported us with of guidance, and we desire His help in what we have intended and His confirmation, for He is over all things a Witness; and we ask Him to bless Muhammad the best of what He has blessed any of His chosen creation, and to grant him his request, and to join us to him by His bounty, for He is Rich, Generous. The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: To proceed, I have reflected on the cause of the disagreement of humans concerning the world after the appearance of signs of its origination and proofs that the management of another runs upon it. For there is nothing from the substances (jawahir) of the world and its pillars except that it testifies by its substance that it is managed and created (madbar mafṭur); and that it is in need of a Knower of its states, Rich who possesses its needs, Wise who places everything in its place; lest it contradict and become dissipated; and that it cannot by its substance revert to a number of managers, given that with them is the possibility of disagreement wherein each one wants to manifest his authority, and overcome his kingdom, and subdue every contender, and in that is annihilation and corruption. Unless one of them has an excess of power or support to which all submit, then every one becomes submissive and humbled to him: meaning every substance of the world is in its emergence dependent on the will of another and his authority over it. And that is what is meant by that which is a proof of a manager of the world, Knowing, Wise, so that by him it may stand and be completed and come out from non-existence into existence, for the wonder of the need in its beginning for one who knows the manner of creating things is not less than the wonder of its continuance and standing as it is. Rather, it was more apparent, and the need in that for another is greater; for it is more incapable of managing itself, and the causes of its being changed upon it are more apparent. Along with what is in all proofs of its existence after it was not is more evident, for every possessor of intellect and sight perhaps remembers its beginning or its change from states that preceded of smallness and delicacy, such that if that whole is not given a beginning, its existence is invalidated. Then the possibility of all weakness and frailty until it perishes and becomes invalid compels it to knowledge of its existence after it was not. And if it is a matter that belongs to one who possesses management and knows states, then the dead—which is under the management of the living, they benefit from it without it perceiving that—is more deserving of that. Then the existence of the dead for what the living have of enjoyment indicates that the One who managed them is the One who managed the living, since He made them a means of enjoyment for them, by them their well-being.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage begins Maturidi's argument for the existence and oneness of God. It is a proof-by-design argument. The author first states the problem: despite clear signs of the world's origination (it being created) and evidence that it is managed by another, humans disagree. He then asserts that every substance (jawhar) and pillar of the world testifies by its very nature that it is created and managed (madbar mafṭur). This testimony implies that the world needs a Creator who knows its conditions, is rich (independent) to fulfill its needs, and is wise to place everything appropriately, preventing contradiction and decay. He then argues against polytheism: if there were multiple managers, their different wills would lead to conflict and ruin, unless one dominates—but then that one is the true manager. So the world's coherence points to a single Wise Manager. He further argues that the world's origination from non-existence is even more wondrous than its continued existence, and its ongoing weakness and decay prove it had a beginning and depends on another. He then uses the example of dead matter being used by the living to show that the same wise manager controls both the living and the dead for the benefit of the living. The argument flows from the observed order and dependency to the necessity of a single, wise, eternal Creator.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
مدبر (madbar)
مفطور (mafṭur)
اختصاف (ikhtilaf)
تفاني (fana')
فساد (fasād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the testimony is metaphorical or only applies to certain objects.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly says 'there is nothing from the substances... except that it testifies by its substance'—this is a universal, inherent testimony.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why can there not be multiple managers of the world?
A) They would be too busy to manage the world
B) Their different wills would lead to contradiction and corruption
C) The world is too small for multiple managers
D) Humans cannot perceive more than one manager

Key Takeaway
The very nature of every substance in the world testifies that it is created and managed, demanding a single wise Creator who knows, is independent, and is wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every substance in the universe testifies that it is managed, have you ever truly reflected on the fact that your own existence—your breath, your heartbeat—is a constant testimony of a Manager? What would change if you lived with this awareness every moment?
?
When you look at your own life, do you see the hand of a single Wise Manager, or do you sometimes act as if your life is governed by multiple competing forces? What would it mean to submit fully to that one Manager?
?
The passage says that if there were multiple managers, the world would fall into corruption and annihilation. In your own experience, have you felt the chaos of trying to serve multiple masters? How does this passage call you to single-hearted devotion?

The Greater Wonder of the World’s Origination
Maturidi argues that the world's origination is a greater proof of a Creator than its continuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the world’s beginning from non-existence is an even stronger proof of a Creator than its continued existence.

Core Idea
The world’s beginning requires a Creator more than its continuation.

Having established that the world is managed, Maturidi now focuses on its origination. He argues that the wonder of the need for a Creator at the beginning is greater than the wonder of its continuation. Moreover, the world’s constant change and decay prove it had a beginning and is dependent on another. This argument reinforces the necessity of a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the comparison Maturidi makes between the beginning and continuation of the world.
The phrase 'al-aʿjūbah' (wonder) is key.
2
Identify the main argument
Maturidi says the wonder of the need for a creator at the beginning is not less than the wonder of its continuance; rather, it is more apparent. Understand why this strengthens the proof.
He emphasizes that the world is incapable of managing itself.
3
Notice the evidence from decay
Maturidi points to the world's weakness and change from states (smallness, delicacy) as evidence that it had a beginning and is dependent. Reflect on how this supports his argument.
The decay proves the world is not eternal.

?

If not, what is holding you back from making that reflection a regular part of your worship?
?

The Living and the Dead Under One Manager
Maturidi uses the example of dead matter serving the living to prove one Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the relationship between living beings and dead matter to prove that one wise Creator manages both.

Core Idea
The same Manager who controls the living also controls the dead.

In the final part of his argument, Maturidi presents an analogy: dead matter is used by the living for their benefit, yet the dead matter does not manage itself. This shows that the one who manages the living also manages the dead. Thus, the entire world, living and non-living, is under the management of a single wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between 'al-mawāt' (dead) and 'al-aḥyā' (living).
The key point is: the dead are managed by the living, but that itself points to a higher manager.
2
Understand the analogy
Maturidi argues that if a dead thing is under the management of the living and they benefit from it, and the dead thing does not manage itself, then this indicates that the same wise manager who created the living also created and manages the dead.
This unifies the argument: both living and non-living testify to one Creator.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اختلاف البشر في العالم
نبتدأ بالحمد لله العلي الحميد، ونتوجه إليه بالشكر له والتمجيد على ما أيدنا به من التسديد، ونرغب إليه في العون على ما قصدنا له والتأييد، فإنه على كل شيء شهيد؛ ونسأله أن يصلي على محمد أفضل ما صلى على أحد من خيار خلقه، وأن يعطيه سؤله، وأن يلحقنا به بجوده، فإنه غني كريم.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : أما بعد، فإني تأملت وجه اختلاف البشر في العالم بعد ظهور آيات حدثه، وأدلة جرى تدبير غيره عليه. إذ ما من شيء من جواهر العالم وأركانه إلا وهو بجوهره يشهد بأنه مدبر مفطور؛ وأنه مضطر إلى عليم بأحواله، غني يملك حوائجه، حكيم يضع كل شيء موضعه؛ لئلا يتناقض فيتبدد؛ وأنه لا يحتمل بجوهره أن يرجع إلى عدد من المدبرين، بما لديه تمكن الاختلاف الذي عنده يريد كل أن يظهر سلطانه، ويغلب ملكه، ويقهر كل من نازعه، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد. اللهم إلا أن يكون لواحد منهم فضل قوة أو نصر يخضع له الجميع، فيصير كل خاضعا له ذليلاً: بمعنى كل جوهر من جواهر العالم يكون في خروجه على مشيئة غيره وجزيه عليه سلطانه. وهو المعني بالذي هو دليل مدبر للعالم، عليم حكيم، ليقوم به هو ويتم ويخرج من العدم إلى الوجود، إذ الأعجوبة التي الحاجة في ابتداء كونه إلى من يعلم كيفية إنشاء الأشياء ليست بدون الأعجوبة في دوامه وقيامه على ما هو عليه . بل كانت أظهر، والحاجة في ذلك إلى غيره أعظم؛ إذ هو عن تدبير نفسه أعجز، وأسباب إحالته عليه أظهر . مع ما في كل براهين كونه بعد أن لم يكن أبين، إذ كل ذي عقل وبصر لعلّه يذكر ابتداءه أو تقلبه من أحوال تقدمت من الصغر واللطافة، مما إذا لم يجعل لتلك الجملة ابتداء يبطل كونه . ثم احتمال كل الوهن والضعف إلى أن يتلاشى ويبطل مما يضطره إلى العلم بكونه بعد أن لم يكن . وإذا كان ذا أمر من يملك التدبير ويعلم بالأحوال فالموات ـ الذي هو تحت تدبير الأحياء ينتفعون به من حيث لا يشعر بذلك ـ أحق بذلك . ثم دل كون الأموات على ما للأحياء بها الاستمتاع، على أن الذي دبرها هو الذي دبر الأحياء، إذ جعلها مستمتعا لهم، بها صلاحهم.
Question: The disagreement of humans concerning the world. We begin with praise to Allah, the High, the Praised, and we turn to Him with thanks and glorification for what He has supported us with of guidance, and we desire His help in what we have intended and His confirmation, for He is over all things a Witness; and we ask Him to bless Muhammad the best of what He has blessed any of His chosen creation, and to grant him his request, and to join us to him by His bounty, for He is Rich, Generous. The jurist Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: To proceed, I have reflected on the cause of the disagreement of humans concerning the world after the appearance of signs of its origination and proofs that the management of another runs upon it. For there is nothing from the substances (jawahir) of the world and its pillars except that it testifies by its substance that it is managed and created (madbar mafṭur); and that it is in need of a Knower of its states, Rich who possesses its needs, Wise who places everything in its place; lest it contradict and become dissipated; and that it cannot by its substance revert to a number of managers, given that with them is the possibility of disagreement wherein each one wants to manifest his authority, and overcome his kingdom, and subdue every contender, and in that is annihilation and corruption. Unless one of them has an excess of power or support to which all submit, then every one becomes submissive and humbled to him: meaning every substance of the world is in its emergence dependent on the will of another and his authority over it. And that is what is meant by that which is a proof of a manager of the world, Knowing, Wise, so that by him it may stand and be completed and come out from non-existence into existence, for the wonder of the need in its beginning for one who knows the manner of creating things is not less than the wonder of its continuance and standing as it is. Rather, it was more apparent, and the need in that for another is greater; for it is more incapable of managing itself, and the causes of its being changed upon it are more apparent. Along with what is in all proofs of its existence after it was not is more evident, for every possessor of intellect and sight perhaps remembers its beginning or its change from states that preceded of smallness and delicacy, such that if that whole is not given a beginning, its existence is invalidated. Then the possibility of all weakness and frailty until it perishes and becomes invalid compels it to knowledge of its existence after it was not. And if it is a matter that belongs to one who possesses management and knows states, then the dead—which is under the management of the living, they benefit from it without it perceiving that—is more deserving of that. Then the existence of the dead for what the living have of enjoyment indicates that the One who managed them is the One who managed the living, since He made them a means of enjoyment for them, by them their well-being.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage begins Maturidi's argument for the existence and oneness of God. It is a proof-by-design argument. The author first states the problem: despite clear signs of the world's origination (it being created) and evidence that it is managed by another, humans disagree. He then asserts that every substance (jawhar) and pillar of the world testifies by its very nature that it is created and managed (madbar mafṭur). This testimony implies that the world needs a Creator who knows its conditions, is rich (independent) to fulfill its needs, and is wise to place everything appropriately, preventing contradiction and decay. He then argues against polytheism: if there were multiple managers, their different wills would lead to conflict and ruin, unless one dominates—but then that one is the true manager. So the world's coherence points to a single Wise Manager. He further argues that the world's origination from non-existence is even more wondrous than its continued existence, and its ongoing weakness and decay prove it had a beginning and depends on another. He then uses the example of dead matter being used by the living to show that the same wise manager controls both the living and the dead for the benefit of the living. The argument flows from the observed order and dependency to the necessity of a single, wise, eternal Creator.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt)
أحياء (aḥyā')
استمتاع (istimtā')
صلاح (ṣalāḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that living beings benefit from dead matter?
A) The dead are independent of any manager
B) The same manager who created the living also created the dead
C) The living created the dead
D) The dead manage themselves

Key Takeaway
The fact that living beings use and benefit from dead matter shows that one wise Creator manages both the living and the non-living.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Когда вы ежедневно пользуетесь природными ресурсами (мёртвой материей), осознаёте ли вы, что они созданы для вашего блага Тем же Творцом, Который дал вам жизнь? Как это меняет вашу благодарность?
?
Если всё, от почвы до воздуха, находится под управлением одного Существа, что это говорит о вашем собственном управлении своей жизнью? Соответствуете ли вы Его замыслу?
?
Мёртвая материя не осознаёт ту пользу, которую приносит вам. Сколько благ, которыми вы пользуетесь, вы не осознаёте? Как вы можете стать более осознанным по отношению к Управляющему за ними?
